<p><b>Karl Heinrich Marx</b> <span class="noexcerpt nowraplinks" style="font-size:small; font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Fellow_of_the_Royal_Society_of_Arts" title="Fellow of the Royal Society of Arts">FRSA</a></span> (<small>German: </small><span class="IPA" lang="de-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[maʁks]</a></span>; 5 May 1818 – 14 March 1883) was a German philosopher, economist, historian, sociologist, political theorist, journalist, <a href="/wiki/Critique_of_political_economy" title="Critique of political economy">critic of political economy</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socialist_revolutionary" title="Socialist revolutionary">socialist revolutionary</a>. His best-known titles are the 1848 pamphlet <i><a href="/wiki/The_Communist_Manifesto" title="The Communist Manifesto">The Communist Manifesto</a></i> and the four-volume <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Das_Kapital" title="Das Kapital">Das Kapital</a></i></span> (1867–1883). Marx's political and philosophical thought had enormous influence on subsequent intellectual, economic, and political history. His name has been used as an adjective, a noun, and a school of social theory.
</p><p>Born in <a href="/wiki/Trier" title="Trier">Trier</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_Confederation" title="German Confederation">Germany</a>, Marx studied law and philosophy at the universities of <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bonn" title="University of Bonn">Bonn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Humboldt_University_of_Berlin" title="Humboldt University of Berlin">Berlin</a>. He married German theatre critic and political activist <a href="/wiki/Jenny_von_Westphalen" title="Jenny von Westphalen">Jenny von Westphalen</a> in 1843. Due to his political publications, Marx became <a href="/wiki/Statelessness" title="Statelessness">stateless</a> and lived in exile with his wife and children in London for decades, where he continued to develop his thought in collaboration with German philosopher <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a> and publish his writings, researching in the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum_Reading_Room" title="British Museum Reading Room">British Museum Reading Room</a>.
</p><p>Marx's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_theories" title="Critical theories">critical theories</a> about society, economics, and politics, collectively understood as <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>, hold that human societies develop through <a href="/wiki/Class_conflict" title="Class conflict">class conflict</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Capitalist_mode_of_production_(Marxist_theory)" title="Capitalist mode of production (Marxist theory)">capitalist mode of production</a>, this manifests itself in the conflict between the <a href="/wiki/Ruling_class" title="Ruling class">ruling classes</a> (known as the <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeoisie</a>) that control the <a href="/wiki/Means_of_production" title="Means of production">means of production</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Working_class" title="Working class">working classes</a> (known as the <a href="/wiki/Proletariat" title="Proletariat">proletariat</a>) that enable these means by selling their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labour-power" title="Labour-power">labour-power</a> in return for wages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manifesto_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-manifesto-3">[3]</a></sup> Employing a critical approach known as <a href="/wiki/Historical_materialism" title="Historical materialism">historical materialism</a>, Marx predicted that capitalism produced <a href="/wiki/Internal_contradictions_of_capital_accumulation" title="Internal contradictions of capital accumulation">internal tensions</a> like previous socioeconomic systems and that those would lead to its self-destruction and replacement by a new system known as the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_mode_of_production" title="Socialist mode of production">socialist mode of production</a>. For Marx, class antagonisms under capitalism—owing in part to its instability and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_crisis" title="Economic crisis">crisis</a>-prone nature—would eventuate the working class's development of <a href="/wiki/Class_consciousness" title="Class consciousness">class consciousness</a>, leading to their conquest of political power and eventually the establishment of a <a href="/wiki/Classless_society" title="Classless society">classless</a>, <a href="/wiki/Communist_society" title="Communist society">communist society</a> constituted by a <a href="/wiki/Free_association_of_producers" title="Free association of producers">free association of producers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Marx actively pressed for its implementation, arguing that the working class should carry out organised <a href="/wiki/Proletarian_revolution" title="Proletarian revolution">proletarian revolutionary</a> action to topple capitalism and bring about socio-economic <a href="/wiki/Emancipation" title="Emancipation">emancipation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-23-24_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-23-24-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marx has been described as one of the most influential figures in <a href="/wiki/Human_history" title="Human history">human history</a>, and his work has been both lauded and <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Marxism" title="Criticism of Marxism">criticised</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manchester_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manchester-6">[6]</a></sup> His work in economics laid the basis for some current theories about labour and its relation to <a href="/wiki/Capital_(economics)" title="Capital (economics)">capital</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Many intellectuals, labour unions, artists, and political parties worldwide have been influenced by Marx's work, with many modifying or adapting his ideas. Marx is typically cited as one of the principal architects of modern <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social science</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Childhood_and_early_education:_1818.E2.80.931836"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_and_early_education:_1818–1836">Childhood and early education: 1818–1836</span></h3>
<p>Karl Heinrich Marx was born on 5 May 1818 to <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Marx" title="Heinrich Marx">Heinrich Marx</a> (1777–1838) and <a href="/wiki/Henriette_Pressburg" title="Henriette Pressburg">Henriette Pressburg</a> (1788–1863). He was born at Brückengasse 664 in <a href="/wiki/Trier" title="Trier">Trier</a>, an ancient city then part of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Prussia" title="Kingdom of Prussia">Kingdom of Prussia</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_the_Lower_Rhine" title="Grand Duchy of the Lower Rhine">Province of the Lower Rhine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Marx's family was originally <a href="/wiki/Jewish_secularism" title="Jewish secularism">non-religious Jewish</a>, but <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Christianity" title="Conversion to Christianity">had converted formally to Christianity</a> before his birth. His maternal grandfather was a Dutch <a href="/wiki/Rabbi" title="Rabbi">rabbi</a>, while his paternal line had supplied Trier's rabbis since 1723, a role taken by his grandfather Meier Halevi Marx.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> His father, as a child known as Herschel, was the first in the line to receive a secular education. He became a lawyer with a comfortably <a href="/wiki/Upper_middle_class" title="Upper middle class">upper middle class</a> income and the family owned a number of <a href="/wiki/Moselle_wine" title="Moselle wine">Moselle</a> vineyards, in addition to his income as an attorney. Prior to his son's birth and after the abrogation of <a href="/wiki/Jewish_emancipation" title="Jewish emancipation">Jewish emancipation</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Rhineland" title="Rhineland">Rhineland</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Herschel converted from Judaism to join the state <a href="/wiki/Prussian_Union_of_Churches" title="Prussian Union of Churches">Evangelical Church of Prussia</a>, taking on the German forename Heinrich over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yiddish_language" title="Yiddish language">Yiddish</a> Herschel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trier_BW_2014-06-21_11-11-49.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4795" data-file-width="5161" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Trier_BW_2014-06-21_11-11-49.jpg/220px-Trier_BW_2014-06-21_11-11-49.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Trier_BW_2014-06-21_11-11-49.jpg/330px-Trier_BW_2014-06-21_11-11-49.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Trier_BW_2014-06-21_11-11-49.jpg/440px-Trier_BW_2014-06-21_11-11-49.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trier_BW_2014-06-21_11-11-49.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karl-Marx-Haus" title="Karl-Marx-Haus">Marx's birthplace</a>, now Brückenstraße 10, in Trier. The family occupied two rooms on the ground floor and three on the first floor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Purchased by the <a href="/wiki/Social_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="Social Democratic Party of Germany">Social Democratic Party of Germany</a> in 1928, it now houses a museum devoted to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Largely non-religious, Heinrich was a man of the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>, interested in the ideas of the philosophers <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_liberal" title="Classical liberal">classical liberal</a>, he took part in agitation for a constitution and reforms in Prussia, which was then an <a href="/wiki/Absolute_monarchy" title="Absolute monarchy">absolute monarchy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In 1815, Heinrich Marx began working as an attorney and in 1819 moved his family to a ten-room property near the <a href="/wiki/Porta_Nigra" title="Porta Nigra">Porta Nigra</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> His wife, Henriette Pressburg, was a Dutch Jew from a prosperous business family that later founded the company <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philips_Electronics" title="Philips Electronics">Philips Electronics</a>. Her sister Sophie Pressburg (1797–1854) married <a href="/wiki/Lion_Philips" title="Lion Philips">Lion Philips</a> (1794–1866) and was the grandmother of both <a href="/wiki/Gerard_Philips" title="Gerard Philips">Gerard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anton_Philips" title="Anton Philips">Anton Philips</a> and great-grandmother to <a href="/wiki/Frits_Philips" title="Frits Philips">Frits Philips</a>. Lion Philips was a wealthy Dutch tobacco manufacturer and industrialist, upon whom Karl and <a href="/wiki/Jenny_von_Westphalen" title="Jenny von Westphalen">Jenny Marx</a> would later often come to rely for loans while they were exiled in London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Little is known of Marx's childhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The third of nine children, he became the eldest son when his brother Moritz died in 1819.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Marx and his surviving siblings, Sophie, Hermann, Henriette, Louise, Emilie, and Caroline, were <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptised</a> into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutheran_Church" title="Lutheran Church">Lutheran Church</a> in August 1824, and their mother in November 1825.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Marx was privately educated by his father until 1830 when he entered Trier High School (<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich-Wilhelm-Gymnasium_(Trier)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Friedrich-Wilhelm-Gymnasium (Trier) (page does not exist)">Gymnasium zu Trier</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich-Wilhelm-Gymnasium_(Trier)" title="de:Friedrich-Wilhelm-Gymnasium (Trier)">de</a>]</span>), whose headmaster, Hugo Wyttenbach, was a friend of his father. By employing many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liberal_humanism" title="Liberal humanism">liberal humanists</a> as teachers, Wyttenbach incurred the anger of the local conservative government. Subsequently, police raided the school in 1832 and discovered that literature espousing political liberalism was being distributed among the students. Considering the distribution of such material a seditious act, the authorities instituted reforms and replaced several staff during Marx's attendance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 1835 at the age of 17, Marx travelled to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Bonn" title="University of Bonn">University of Bonn</a> wishing to study philosophy and literature, but his father insisted on law as a more practical field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Due to a condition referred to as a "weak chest",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Marx was excused from military duty when he turned 18. While at the University at Bonn, Marx joined the Poets' Club, a group containing political radicals that were monitored by the police.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Marx also joined the Trier Tavern Club drinking society (German: <i lang="de">Landsmannschaft der Treveraner</i>) where many ideas were discussed and at one point he served as the club's co-president.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drinking1_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-drinking1-29">[29]</a></sup> Additionally, Marx was involved in certain disputes, some of which became serious: in August 1836 he took part in a duel with a member of the university's <a href="/wiki/Corps_Borussia_Bonn" title="Corps Borussia Bonn">Borussian Korps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Although his grades in the first term were good, they soon deteriorated, leading his father to force a transfer to the more serious and academic <a href="/wiki/Humboldt_University_of_Berlin" title="Humboldt University of Berlin">University of Berlin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Hegelianism_and_early_journalism:_1836.E2.80.931843"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hegelianism_and_early_journalism:_1836–1843">Hegelianism and early journalism: 1836–1843</span></h3>

Related categories</th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">
<div class="CategoryTreeTag" data-ct-mode="0" data-ct-options='{"mode":0,"hideprefix":20,"showcount":false,"namespaces":false,"notranslations":false}'><div class="CategoryTreeSection"><div class="CategoryTreeItem"><span class="CategoryTreeBullet"><span class="CategoryTreeToggle" data-ct-state="collapsed" data-ct-title="Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel"></span> </span> <a href="/wiki/Category:Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Category:Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel</a></div><div class="CategoryTreeChildren" style="display:none"></div></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar" style="border-top:1px solid #aaa;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Hegelianism" title="Template:Hegelianism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Hegelianism" title="Template talk:Hegelianism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Hegelianism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Spending summer and autumn 1836 in Trier, Marx became more serious about his studies and his life. He became engaged to <a href="/wiki/Jenny_von_Westphalen" title="Jenny von Westphalen">Jenny von Westphalen</a>, an educated member of the <a href="/wiki/Edler" title="Edler">petty nobility</a> who had known Marx since childhood. As she had broken off her engagement with a young <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy_(class)" title="Aristocracy (class)">aristocrat</a> to be with Marx, their relationship was socially controversial owing to the differences between their religious and class origins, but Marx befriended her father <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_von_Westphalen" title="Ludwig von Westphalen">Ludwig von Westphalen</a> (a liberal aristocrat) and later dedicated his doctoral thesis to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Seven years after their engagement, on 19 June 1843, they married in a Protestant church in <a href="/wiki/Bad_Kreuznach" title="Bad Kreuznach">Kreuznach</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>In October 1836, Marx arrived in Berlin, matriculating in the university's faculty of law and renting a room in the Mittelstrasse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> During the first term, Marx attended lectures of <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Gans" title="Eduard Gans">Eduard Gans</a> (who represented the progressive Hegelian standpoint, elaborated on rational development in history by emphasising particularly its libertarian aspects, and the importance of social question) and of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karl_von_Savigny" title="Karl von Savigny">Karl von Savigny</a> (who represented the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historical_School_of_Law" title="Historical School of Law">Historical School of Law</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Although studying law, he was fascinated by philosophy and looked for a way to combine the two, believing that "without philosophy nothing could be accomplished".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Marx became interested in the recently deceased German philosopher <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel</a>, whose ideas were then widely debated among European philosophical circles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> During a convalescence in Stralau, he joined the Doctor's Club (<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Doktorklub</i></span>), a student group which discussed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegelianism" title="Hegelianism">Hegelian</a> ideas, and through them became involved with a group of <a href="/wiki/Radical_politics" title="Radical politics">radical thinkers</a> known as the <a href="/wiki/Young_Hegelians" title="Young Hegelians">Young Hegelians</a> in 1837. They gathered around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach" title="Ludwig Andreas Feuerbach">Ludwig Feuerbach</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Bauer" title="Bruno Bauer">Bruno Bauer</a>, with Marx developing a particularly close friendship with Adolf Rutenberg. Like Marx, the Young Hegelians were critical of Hegel's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metaphysical" title="Metaphysical">metaphysical</a> assumptions, but adopted his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegelian_Dialectic" title="Hegelian Dialectic">dialectical method</a> to criticise established society, politics and religion from a left-wing perspective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Marx's father died in May 1838, resulting in a diminished income for the family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Marx had been emotionally close to his father and treasured his memory after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jenny_von_Westphalen-1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3380" data-file-width="2829" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Jenny_von_Westphalen-1.jpg/170px-Jenny_von_Westphalen-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Jenny_von_Westphalen-1.jpg/255px-Jenny_von_Westphalen-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Jenny_von_Westphalen-1.jpg/340px-Jenny_von_Westphalen-1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jenny_von_Westphalen-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jenny_von_Westphalen" title="Jenny von Westphalen">Jenny von Westphalen</a> in the 1830s</div></div></div>
<p>By 1837, Marx was writing both fiction and non-fiction, having completed a short novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Scorpion_and_Felix" title="Scorpion and Felix">Scorpion and Felix</a></i>; a drama, <i><a href="/wiki/Oulanem" title="Oulanem">Oulanem</a></i>; as well as a number of love poems dedicated to Jenny von Westphalen. None of this early work was published during his lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The love poems were published posthumously in the <i>Collected Works of Karl Marx and Frederick Engels: Volume 1</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Marx soon abandoned fiction for other pursuits, including the study of both English and Italian, <a href="/wiki/Art_history" title="Art history">art history</a> and the translation of Latin classics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> He began co-operating with <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Bauer" title="Bruno Bauer">Bruno Bauer</a> on editing Hegel's <i><a href="/wiki/Lectures_on_the_Philosophy_of_Religion" title="Lectures on the Philosophy of Religion">Philosophy of Religion</a></i> in 1840. Marx was also engaged in writing his doctoral thesis, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Difference_Between_the_Democritean_and_Epicurean_Philosophy_of_Nature" title="The Difference Between the Democritean and Epicurean Philosophy of Nature">The Difference Between the Democritean and Epicurean Philosophy of Nature</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> which he completed in 1841. It was described as "a daring and original piece of work in which Marx set out to show that theology must yield to the superior wisdom of philosophy".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The essay was controversial, particularly among the conservative professors at the University of Berlin. Marx decided instead to submit his thesis to the more liberal <a href="/wiki/University_of_Jena" title="University of Jena">University of Jena</a>, whose faculty awarded him his <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Philosophy" title="Doctor of Philosophy">Ph.D.</a> in April 1841.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-willamette1_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-willamette1-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> As Marx and Bauer were both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheists" title="Atheists">atheists</a>, in March 1841 they began plans for a journal entitled <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Archiv des Atheismus</i></span> (<i>Atheistic Archives</i>), but it never came to fruition. In July, Marx and Bauer took a trip to <a href="/wiki/Bonn" title="Bonn">Bonn</a> from Berlin. There they scandalised their class by getting drunk, laughing in church and galloping through the streets on donkeys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marx was considering an academic career, but this path was barred by the government's growing opposition to classical liberalism and the Young Hegelians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Marx moved to <a href="/wiki/Cologne" title="Cologne">Cologne</a> in 1842, where he became a journalist, writing for the radical newspaper <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Rheinische_Zeitung" title="Rheinische Zeitung">Rheinische Zeitung</a></i></span> (<i>Rhineland News</i>), expressing his early views on socialism and his developing interest in economics. Marx criticised right-wing European governments as well as figures in the liberal and socialist movements, whom he thought ineffective or counter-productive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The newspaper attracted the attention of the Prussian <a href="/wiki/Censorship" title="Censorship">government censors</a>, who checked every issue for seditious material before printing, as Marx lamented: "Our newspaper has to be presented to the police to be sniffed at, and if the police nose smells anything un-Christian or un-Prussian, the newspaper is not allowed to appear".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> After the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Rheinische_Zeitung" title="Rheinische Zeitung">Rheinische Zeitung</a></i></span> published an article strongly criticising the Russian monarchy, Tsar <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_I_of_Russia" title="Nicholas I of Russia">Nicholas I</a> requested it be banned and Prussia's government complied in 1843.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Paris:_1843.E2.80.931845"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Paris:_1843–1845">Paris: 1843–1845</span></h3>
<p>In 1843, Marx became co-editor of a new, radical left-wing Parisian newspaper, the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deutsch-Franz%C3%B6sische_Jahrb%C3%BCcher" title="Deutsch-Französische Jahrbücher">Deutsch-Französische Jahrbücher</a></i></span> (<i>German-French Annals</i>), then being set up by the German activist <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Ruge" title="Arnold Ruge">Arnold Ruge</a> to bring together German and French radicals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Therefore Marx and his wife moved to Paris in October 1843. Initially living with Ruge and his wife communally at 23 <a href="/wiki/Rue_Vaneau" title="Rue Vaneau">Rue Vaneau</a>, they found the living conditions difficult, so moved out following the birth of their daughter Jenny in 1844.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Although intended to attract writers from both France and the German states, the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Jahrbücher</i></span> was dominated by the latter and the only non-German writer was the exiled Russian <a href="/wiki/Collectivist_anarchism" title="Collectivist anarchism">anarchist collectivist</a> <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Bakunin" title="Mikhail Bakunin">Mikhail Bakunin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Marx contributed two essays to the paper, "<a href="/wiki/Critique_of_Hegel%27s_Philosophy_of_Right" title="Critique of Hegel's Philosophy of Right">Introduction to a Contribution to the Critique of Hegel's Philosophy of Right</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> and "<a href="/wiki/On_the_Jewish_Question" title="On the Jewish Question">On the Jewish Question</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> the latter introducing his belief that the <a href="/wiki/Proletariat" title="Proletariat">proletariat</a> were a revolutionary force and marking his embrace of communism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Only one issue was published, but it was relatively successful, largely owing to the inclusion of <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Heine" title="Heinrich Heine">Heinrich Heine</a>'s satirical odes on King <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_I_of_Bavaria" title="Ludwig I of Bavaria">Ludwig of Bavaria</a>, leading the German states to ban it and seize imported copies (Ruge nevertheless refused to fund the publication of further issues and his friendship with Marx broke down).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> After the paper's collapse, Marx began writing for the only uncensored German-language radical newspaper left, <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Vorw%C3%A4rts!" title="Vorwärts!">Vorwärts!</a></i></span> (<i>Forward!</i>). Based in Paris, the paper was connected to the <a href="/wiki/League_of_the_Just" title="League of the Just">League of the Just</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Utopian_socialism" title="Utopian socialism">utopian socialist</a> secret society of workers and artisans. Marx attended some of their meetings but did not join.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> In <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Vorwärts!</i></span>, Marx refined his views on socialism based upon Hegelian and Feuerbachian ideas of dialectical materialism, at the same time criticising liberals and other socialists operating in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Engels_1856.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="582" data-file-width="474" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Engels_1856.jpg/170px-Engels_1856.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Engels_1856.jpg/255px-Engels_1856.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Engels_1856.jpg/340px-Engels_1856.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Engels_1856.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a>, whom Marx met in 1844; the two became lifelong friends and collaborators.</div></div></div>
<p>On 28 August 1844, Marx met the German socialist <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Caf%C3%A9_de_la_R%C3%A9gence" title="Café de la Régence">Café de la Régence</a>, beginning a lifelong friendship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Engels showed Marx his recently published <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Condition_of_the_Working_Class_in_England_in_1844" title="The Condition of the Working Class in England in 1844">The Condition of the Working Class in England in 1844</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> convincing Marx that the working class would be the agent and instrument of the final revolution in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bottomore1991_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bottomore1991-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197382_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197382-65">[65]</a></sup> Soon, Marx and Engels were collaborating on a criticism of the philosophical ideas of Marx's former friend, <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Bauer" title="Bruno Bauer">Bruno Bauer</a>. This work was published in 1845 as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Holy_Family_(book)" title="The Holy Family (book)">The Holy Family</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Although critical of Bauer, Marx was increasingly influenced by the ideas of the Young Hegelians <a href="/wiki/Max_Stirner" title="Max Stirner">Max Stirner</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Feuerbach" title="Ludwig Feuerbach">Ludwig Feuerbach</a>, but eventually Marx and Engels abandoned Feuerbachian materialism as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-et_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-et-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the time that he lived at 38 Rue Vaneau in Paris (from October 1843 until January 1845),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Marx engaged in an intensive study of <a href="/wiki/Political_economy" title="Political economy">political economy</a> (<a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a>, <a href="/wiki/David_Ricardo" title="David Ricardo">David Ricardo</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Mill" title="James Mill">James Mill</a>, <i>etc.</i>)<i>,</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197363_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197363-70">[70]</a></sup> the French socialists (especially <a href="/wiki/Henri_de_Saint-Simon" title="Henri de Saint-Simon">Claude Henri St. Simon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Fourier" title="Charles Fourier">Charles Fourier</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> and the history of France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197362_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197362-72">[72]</a></sup> The study of, and <a href="/wiki/Critique_of_political_economy" title="Critique of political economy">critique of political economy</a> is a project that Marx would pursue for the rest of his life<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> and would result in his major economic work—the three-volume series called <i>Das Kapital</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a> is based in large part on three influences: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegel" title="Hegel">Hegel</a>'s dialectics, French utopian socialism and British political economy. Together with his earlier study of Hegel's dialectics, the studying that Marx did during this time in Paris meant that all major components of "Marxism" were in place by the autumn of 1844.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Marx was constantly being pulled away from his critique of political economy—not only by the usual daily demands of the time, but additionally by editing a radical newspaper and later by organising and directing the efforts of a political party during years of potentially revolutionary popular uprisings of the citizenry. Still, Marx was always drawn back to his studies where he sought "to understand the inner workings of capitalism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197362_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197362-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>An outline of "Marxism" had definitely formed in the mind of Karl Marx by late 1844. Indeed, many features of the Marxist view of the world had been worked out in great detail, but Marx needed to write down all of the details of his world view to further clarify the new critique of political economy in his own mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Accordingly, Marx wrote <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_and_Philosophical_Manuscripts_of_1844" title="Economic and Philosophical Manuscripts of 1844">The Economic and Philosophical Manuscripts</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> These manuscripts covered numerous topics, detailing Marx's concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alienated_labour" title="Alienated labour">alienated labour</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-sep-78">[78]</a></sup> By the spring of 1845, his continued study of political economy, capital and capitalism had led Marx to the belief that the new <a href="/wiki/Critique_of_political_economy" title="Critique of political economy">critique of political economy</a> he was espousing—that of scientific socialism—needed to be built on the base of a thoroughly developed materialistic view of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197383_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197383-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Economic and Philosophical Manuscripts of 1844</i> had been written between April and August 1844, but soon Marx recognised that the <i>Manuscripts</i> had been influenced by some inconsistent ideas of Ludwig Feuerbach. Accordingly, Marx recognised the need to break with Feuerbach's philosophy in favour of historical materialism, thus a year later (in April 1845) after moving from Paris to Brussels, Marx wrote his eleven "<a href="/wiki/Theses_on_Feuerbach" title="Theses on Feuerbach">Theses on Feuerbach</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The "Theses on Feuerbach" are best known for Thesis 11, which states that "philosophers have only interpreted the world in various ways, the point is to change it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-sep-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> This work contains Marx's criticism of <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a> (for being contemplative), <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">idealism</a> (for reducing practice to theory), and, overall, philosophy (for putting abstract reality above the physical world).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-sep-78">[78]</a></sup> It thus introduced the first glimpse at Marx's <a href="/wiki/Historical_materialism" title="Historical materialism">historical materialism</a>, an argument that the world is changed not by ideas but by actual, physical, material activity and practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-sep-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Engels1999_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Engels1999-82">[82]</a></sup> In 1845, after receiving a request from the Prussian king, the French government shut down <i>Vorwärts!</i>, with the interior minister, <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Guizot" title="François Guizot">François Guizot</a>, expelling Marx from France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheen_2001._p._90_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wheen_2001._p._90-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Brussels:_1845.E2.80.931848"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Brussels:_1845–1848">Brussels: 1845–1848</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Communist-manifesto.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1045" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="296" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Communist-manifesto.png/170px-Communist-manifesto.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Communist-manifesto.png/255px-Communist-manifesto.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Communist-manifesto.png/340px-Communist-manifesto.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Communist-manifesto.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first edition of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Communist_Manifesto" title="The Communist Manifesto">The Manifesto of the Communist Party</a></i>, published in German in 1848</div></div></div>
<p>Unable either to stay in France or to move to Germany, Marx decided to emigrate to Brussels in Belgium in February 1845. However, to stay in Belgium he had to pledge not to publish anything on the subject of contemporary politics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheen_2001._p._90_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wheen_2001._p._90-83">[83]</a></sup> In Brussels, Marx associated with other exiled socialists from across Europe, including <a href="/wiki/Moses_Hess" title="Moses Hess">Moses Hess</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karl_Heinzen" title="Karl Heinzen">Karl Heinzen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Weydemeyer" title="Joseph Weydemeyer">Joseph Weydemeyer</a>. In April 1845, Engels moved from Barmen in Germany to Brussels to join Marx and the growing cadre of members of the League of the Just now seeking home in Brussels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheen_2001._p._90_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wheen_2001._p._90-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Later, <a href="/wiki/Mary_Burns" title="Mary Burns">Mary Burns</a>, Engels' long-time companion, left Manchester, England to join Engels in Brussels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>In mid-July 1845, Marx and Engels left Brussels for England to visit the leaders of the <a href="/wiki/Chartism" title="Chartism">Chartists</a>, a working-class movement in Britain. This was Marx's first trip to England and Engels was an ideal guide for the trip. Engels had already spent two years living in Manchester from November 1842<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> to August 1844.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Not only did Engels already know the English language,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197389_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197389-88">[88]</a></sup> he had also developed a close relationship with many Chartist leaders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197389_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197389-88">[88]</a></sup> Indeed, Engels was serving as a reporter for many Chartist and socialist English newspapers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197389_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197389-88">[88]</a></sup> Marx used the trip as an opportunity to examine the economic resources available for study in various libraries in London and Manchester.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>In collaboration with Engels, Marx also set about writing a book which is often seen as his best treatment of the concept of <a href="/wiki/Historical_materialism" title="Historical materialism">historical materialism</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_German_Ideology" title="The German Ideology">The German Ideology</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> In this work, Marx broke with <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Feuerbach" title="Ludwig Feuerbach">Ludwig Feuerbach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Bauer" title="Bruno Bauer">Bruno Bauer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Max_Stirner" title="Max Stirner">Max Stirner</a> and the rest of the Young Hegelians, while he also broke with <a href="/wiki/Karl_Theodor_Ferdinand_Gr%C3%BCn" title="Karl Theodor Ferdinand Grün">Karl Grün</a> and other "true socialists" whose philosophies were still based in part on "idealism". In <i>German Ideology</i>, Marx and Engels finally completed their philosophy, which was based solely on materialism as the sole motor force in history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197396–97_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev197396–97-91">[91]</a></sup> <i>German Ideology</i> is written in a humorously satirical form, but even this satirical form did not save the work from censorship. Like so many other early writings of his, <i>German Ideology</i> would not be published in Marx's lifetime and would be published only in 1932.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_78-4"><a href="#cite_note-sep-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wk_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-wk-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>After completing <i>German Ideology</i>, Marx turned to a work that was intended to clarify his own position regarding "the theory and tactics" of a truly "revolutionary proletarian movement" operating from the standpoint of a truly "scientific materialist" philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> This work was intended to draw a distinction between the utopian socialists and Marx's own scientific socialist philosophy. Whereas the utopians believed that people must be persuaded one person at a time to join the socialist movement, the way a person must be persuaded to adopt any different belief, Marx knew that people would tend, on most occasions, to act in accordance with their own economic interests, thus appealing to an entire class (the working class in this case) with a broad appeal to the class's best material interest would be the best way to mobilise the broad mass of that class to make a revolution and change society. This was the intent of the new book that Marx was planning, but to get the manuscript past the government censors he called the book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Poverty_of_Philosophy" title="The Poverty of Philosophy">The Poverty of Philosophy</a></i> (1847)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> and offered it as a response to the "petty-bourgeois philosophy" of the French anarchist socialist <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Joseph_Proudhon" title="Pierre-Joseph Proudhon">Pierre-Joseph Proudhon</a> as expressed in his book <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Philosophy_of_Poverty" title="The Philosophy of Poverty">The Philosophy of Poverty</a></i> (1840).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marx%2BFamily_and_Engels.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1998" data-file-width="1422" decoding="async" height="309" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Marx%2BFamily_and_Engels.jpg/220px-Marx%2BFamily_and_Engels.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Marx%2BFamily_and_Engels.jpg/330px-Marx%2BFamily_and_Engels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Marx%2BFamily_and_Engels.jpg/440px-Marx%2BFamily_and_Engels.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marx%2BFamily_and_Engels.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marx with his daughters and Engels</div></div></div>
<p>These books laid the foundation for Marx and Engels's most famous work, a political pamphlet that has since come to be commonly known as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Communist_Manifesto" title="The Communist Manifesto">The Communist Manifesto</a></i>. While residing in Brussels in 1846, Marx continued his association with the secret radical organisation <a href="/wiki/League_of_the_Just" title="League of the Just">League of the Just</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973124_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973124-97">[97]</a></sup> As noted above, Marx thought the League to be just the sort of radical organisation that was needed to spur the working class of Europe toward the mass movement that would bring about a working-class revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> However, to organise the working class into a mass movement the League had to cease its "secret" or "underground" orientation and operate in the open as a political party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Members of the League eventually became persuaded in this regard. Accordingly, in June 1847 the League was reorganised by its membership into a new open "above ground" political society that appealed directly to the working classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973123–125_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973123–125-100">[100]</a></sup> This new open political society was called the Communist League.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973125_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973125-101">[101]</a></sup> Both Marx and Engels participated in drawing up the programme and organisational principles of the new <a href="/wiki/Communist_League" title="Communist League">Communist League</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>In late 1847, Marx and Engels began writing what was to become their most famous work – a programme of action for the <a href="/wiki/Communist_League" title="Communist League">Communist League</a>. Written jointly by Marx and Engels from December 1847 to January 1848, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Communist_Manifesto" title="The Communist Manifesto">The Communist Manifesto</a></i> was first published on 21 February 1848.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> <i>The Communist Manifesto</i> laid out the beliefs of the new Communist League. No longer a secret society, the Communist League wanted to make aims and intentions clear to the general public rather than hiding its beliefs as the League of the Just had been doing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The opening lines of the pamphlet set forth the principal basis of Marxism: "The history of all hitherto existing society is the history of class struggles".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ShillingMellor2001_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-ShillingMellor2001-105">[105]</a></sup> It goes on to examine the antagonisms that Marx claimed were arising in the clashes of interest between the <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeoisie</a> (the wealthy capitalist class) and the <a href="/wiki/Proletariat" title="Proletariat">proletariat</a> (the industrial working class). Proceeding on from this, the <i>Manifesto</i> presents the argument for why the Communist League, as opposed to other socialist and liberal political parties and groups at the time, was truly acting in the interests of the proletariat to overthrow capitalist society and to replace it with socialism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Later that year, Europe experienced a series of protests, rebellions, and often violent upheavals that became known as the <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848" title="Revolutions of 1848">Revolutions of 1848</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheen_2001._p._125_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wheen_2001._p._125-107">[107]</a></sup> In France, <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution_of_1848" title="French Revolution of 1848">a revolution</a> led to the overthrow of the monarchy and the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/French_Second_Republic" title="French Second Republic">French Second Republic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheen_2001._p._125_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wheen_2001._p._125-107">[107]</a></sup> Marx was supportive of such activity and having recently received a substantial inheritance from his father (withheld by his uncle Lionel Philips since his father's death in 1838) of either 6,000<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaltsevN.-93_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-MaltsevN.-93-108">[108]</a></sup> or 5,000 francs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-110">[110]</a></sup> he allegedly used a third of it to arm Belgian workers who were planning revolutionary action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-110">[110]</a></sup> Although the veracity of these allegations is disputed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MaltsevN.-93_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-MaltsevN.-93-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> the Belgian Ministry of Justice accused Marx of it, subsequently arresting him and he was forced to flee back to France, where with a new republican government in power he believed that he would be safe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Cologne:_1848.E2.80.931849"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cologne:_1848–1849">Cologne: 1848–1849</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marx_and_Engels_at_the_Rheinische_Zeitung.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1047" data-file-width="1492" decoding="async" height="211" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Marx_and_Engels_at_the_Rheinische_Zeitung.jpg/300px-Marx_and_Engels_at_the_Rheinische_Zeitung.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Marx_and_Engels_at_the_Rheinische_Zeitung.jpg/450px-Marx_and_Engels_at_the_Rheinische_Zeitung.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Marx_and_Engels_at_the_Rheinische_Zeitung.jpg/600px-Marx_and_Engels_at_the_Rheinische_Zeitung.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marx_and_Engels_at_the_Rheinische_Zeitung.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marx and Engels in the printing house of the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Neue_Rheinische_Zeitung" title="Neue Rheinische Zeitung">Neue Rheinische Zeitung</a></i></span>. E. Capiro, 1895.</div></div></div>
<p>Temporarily settling down in Paris, Marx transferred the Communist League executive headquarters to the city and also set up a <a href="/wiki/German_Workers%27_Society" title="German Workers' Society">German Workers' Club</a> with various German socialists living there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Hoping to see the revolution spread to Germany, in 1848 Marx moved back to Cologne where he began issuing a handbill entitled the <i>Demands of the Communist Party in Germany</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> in which he argued for only four of the ten points of the <i>Communist Manifesto</i>, believing that in Germany at that time the bourgeoisie must overthrow the <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudal</a> monarchy and aristocracy before the proletariat could overthrow the bourgeoisie.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> On 1 June, Marx started the publication of a daily newspaper, the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de"><a href="/wiki/Neue_Rheinische_Zeitung" title="Neue Rheinische Zeitung">Neue Rheinische Zeitung</a></i></span>, which he helped to finance through his recent inheritance from his father. Designed to put forward news from across Europe with his own Marxist interpretation of events, the newspaper featured Marx as a primary writer and the dominant editorial influence. Despite contributions by fellow members of the Communist League, according to <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a> it remained "a simple dictatorship by Marx".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DL-Marx_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-DL-Marx-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>Whilst editor of the paper, Marx and the other revolutionary socialists were regularly harassed by the police and Marx was brought to trial on several occasions, facing various allegations including insulting the Chief Public Prosecutor, committing a press misdemeanor and inciting armed rebellion through tax boycotting,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicolaievsky2007_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nicolaievsky2007-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Splichal2002_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Splichal2002-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mehring2003_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mehring2003-123">[123]</a></sup> although each time he was acquitted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicolaievsky2007_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nicolaievsky2007-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mehring2003_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mehring2003-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Meanwhile, the democratic parliament in <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a> collapsed and the king, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frederick_William_IV" title="Frederick William IV">Frederick William IV</a>, introduced a new cabinet of his reactionary supporters, who implemented counterrevolutionary measures to expunge left-wing and other revolutionary elements from the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-120">[120]</a></sup> Consequently, the <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Neue Rheinische Zeitung</i></span> was soon suppressed and Marx was ordered to leave the country on 16 May.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DL-Marx_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-DL-Marx-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Marx returned to Paris, which was then under the grip of both a reactionary counterrevolution and a <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> epidemic, and was soon expelled by the city authorities, who considered him a political threat. With his wife Jenny expecting their fourth child and with Marx not able to move back to Germany or Belgium, in August 1849 he sought refuge in London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson2010_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson2010-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Move_to_London_and_further_writing:_1850.E2.80.931860"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Move_to_London_and_further_writing:_1850–1860">Move to London and further writing: 1850–1860</span></h3>
<p>Marx moved to London in early June 1849 and would remain based in the city for the rest of his life. The headquarters of the Communist League also moved to London. However, in the winter of 1849–1850, a split within the ranks of the Communist League occurred when a faction within it led by <a href="/wiki/August_Willich" title="August Willich">August Willich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Schapper" title="Karl Schapper">Karl Schapper</a> began agitating for an immediate uprising. Willich and Schapper believed that once the Communist League had initiated the uprising, the entire working class from across Europe would rise "spontaneously" to join it, thus creating revolution across Europe. Marx and Engels protested that such an unplanned uprising on the part of the Communist League was "adventuristic" and would be suicide for the Communist League.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973233_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973233-128">[128]</a></sup> Such an uprising as that recommended by the Schapper/Willich group would easily be crushed by the police and the armed forces of the reactionary governments of Europe. Marx maintained that this would spell doom for the Communist League itself, arguing that changes in society are not achieved overnight through the efforts and will power of a handful of men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973233_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973233-128">[128]</a></sup> They are instead brought about through a scientific analysis of economic conditions of society and by moving toward revolution through different stages of social development. In the present stage of development (<i>circa</i> 1850), following the defeat of the uprisings across Europe in 1848 he felt that the Communist League should encourage the working class to unite with progressive elements of the rising bourgeoisie to defeat the feudal aristocracy on issues involving demands for governmental reforms, such as a constitutional republic with freely elected assemblies and universal (male) suffrage. In other words, the working class must join with bourgeois and democratic forces to bring about the successful conclusion of the bourgeois revolution before stressing the working class agenda and a working-class revolution.
</p><p>After a long struggle that threatened to ruin the Communist League, Marx's opinion prevailed and eventually, the Willich/Schapper group left the Communist League. Meanwhile, Marx also became heavily involved with the socialist German Workers' Educational Society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> The Society held their meetings in <a href="/wiki/Great_Windmill_Street" title="Great Windmill Street">Great Windmill Street</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soho" title="Soho">Soho</a>, central London's entertainment district.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harriss2006_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harriss2006-131">[131]</a></sup> This organisation was also racked by an internal struggle between its members, some of whom followed Marx while others followed the Schapper/Willich faction. The issues in this internal split were the same issues raised in the internal split within the Communist League, but Marx lost the fight with the Schapper/Willich faction within the German Workers' Educational Society and on 17 September 1850 resigned from the Society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New-York_Daily_Tribune_and_journalism"><i>New-York Daily Tribune</i> and journalism</span></h3>
<p>In the early period in London, Marx committed himself almost exclusively to his studies, such that his family endured extreme poverty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-egsbio_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-egsbio-134">[134]</a></sup> His main source of income was Engels, whose own source was his wealthy industrialist father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-egsbio_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-egsbio-134">[134]</a></sup> In Prussia as editor of his own newspaper, and contributor to others ideologically aligned, Marx could reach his audience, the working classes. In London, without finances to run a newspaper themselves, he and Engels turned to international journalism. At one stage they were being published by six newspapers from England, the United States, <a href="/wiki/Prussia" title="Prussia">Prussia</a>, Austria, and South Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Marx's principal earnings came from his work as European correspondent, from 1852 to 1862, for the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New-York_Daily_Tribune" title="New-York Daily Tribune">New-York Daily Tribune</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kluger_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kluger-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 17">: 17 </span></sup> and from also producing articles for more "bourgeois" newspapers. Marx had his articles translated from German by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Wilhelm_Pieper_(revolutionary)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Wilhelm Pieper (revolutionary) (page does not exist)">Wilhelm Pieper</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_Pieper_(Revolution%C3%A4r)" title="de:Wilhelm Pieper (Revolutionär)">de</a>]</span>, until his proficiency in English had become adequate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dispatches_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dispatches-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>New-York Daily Tribune</i> had been founded in April 1841 by <a href="/wiki/Horace_Greeley" title="Horace Greeley">Horace Greeley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973274_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973274-138">[138]</a></sup> Its editorial board contained progressive bourgeois journalists and publishers, among them <a href="/wiki/George_Ripley_(transcendentalist)" title="George Ripley (transcendentalist)">George Ripley</a> and the journalist <a href="/wiki/Charles_Anderson_Dana" title="Charles Anderson Dana">Charles Dana</a>, who was editor-in-chief. Dana, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fourierist" title="Fourierist">fourierist</a> and an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abolitionist" title="Abolitionist">abolitionist</a>, was Marx's contact. The <i>Tribune</i> was a vehicle for Marx to reach a transatlantic public, such as for his "hidden warfare" against <a href="/wiki/Henry_Charles_Carey" title="Henry Charles Carey">Henry Charles Carey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> The journal had wide working-class appeal from its foundation; at two cents, it was inexpensive;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> and, with about 50,000 copies per issue, its circulation was the widest in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kluger_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kluger-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 14">: 14 </span></sup> Its editorial ethos was progressive and its anti-slavery stance reflected Greeley's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kluger_136-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kluger-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 82">: 82 </span></sup> Marx's first article for the paper, on the British parliamentary elections, was published on 21 August 1852.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 21 March 1857, Dana informed Marx that due to the economic recession only one article a week would be paid for, published or not; the others would be paid for only if published. Marx had sent his articles on Tuesdays and Fridays, but, that October, the <i>Tribune</i> discharged all its correspondents in Europe except Marx and B. Taylor, and reduced Marx to a weekly article. Between September and November 1860, only five were published. After a six-month interval, Marx resumed contributions from September 1861 until March 1862, when Dana wrote to inform him that there was no longer space in the <i>Tribune</i> for reports from London, due to American domestic affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MECW_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-MECW-142">[142]</a></sup>
In 1868, Dana set up a rival newspaper, the <i>New York Sun</i>, at which he was editor-in-chief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> In April 1857, Dana invited Marx to contribute articles, mainly on military history, to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_American_Cyclopedia" title="New American Cyclopedia">New American Cyclopedia</a></i>, an idea of George Ripley, Dana's friend and literary editor of the <i>Tribune</i>. In all, 67 Marx-Engels articles were published, of which 51 were written by Engels, although Marx did some research for them in the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> By the late 1850s, American popular interest in European affairs waned and Marx's articles turned to topics such as the "slavery crisis" and the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a> in 1861 in the "War Between the States".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> Between December 1851 and March 1852, Marx worked on his theoretical work about the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution_of_1848" title="French Revolution of 1848">French Revolution of 1848</a>, titled <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Eighteenth_Brumaire_of_Louis_Napoleon" title="The Eighteenth Brumaire of Louis Napoleon">The Eighteenth Brumaire of Louis Napoleon</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> In this he explored concepts in <a href="/wiki/Historical_materialism" title="Historical materialism">historical materialism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Class_struggle" title="Class struggle">class struggle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dictatorship_of_the_proletariat" title="Dictatorship of the proletariat">dictatorship of the proletariat</a>, and victory of the proletariat over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bourgeois" title="Bourgeois">bourgeois</a> state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marx2008_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marx2008-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1850s and 1860s may be said to mark a philosophical boundary distinguishing the <a href="/wiki/Young_Marx" title="Young Marx">young Marx</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegelian" title="Hegelian">Hegelian</a> <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">idealism</a> and the more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mature_Marx" title="Mature Marx">mature Marx</a>'s<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-148">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood1993_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wood1993-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hook1994_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hook1994-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnston2000_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston2000-151">[151]</a></sup> scientific ideology associated with <a href="/wiki/Structural_Marxism" title="Structural Marxism">structural Marxism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnston2000_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Johnston2000-151">[151]</a></sup> However, not all scholars accept this distinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hook1994_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hook1994-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GeorgeScanlan1975_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-GeorgeScanlan1975-152">[152]</a></sup> For Marx and Engels, their experience of the <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848" title="Revolutions of 1848">Revolutions of 1848</a> to 1849 were formative in the development of their theory of economics and historical progression. After the "failures" of 1848, the revolutionary impetus appeared spent and not to be renewed without an economic recession. Contention arose between Marx and his fellow communists, whom he denounced as "adventurists". Marx deemed it fanciful to propose that "will power" could be sufficient to create the revolutionary conditions when in reality the economic component was the necessary requisite. The recession in the United States' economy in 1852 gave Marx and Engels grounds for optimism for revolutionary activity, yet this economy was seen as too immature for a capitalist revolution. Open territories on America's western frontier dissipated the forces of social unrest. Moreover, any economic crisis arising in the United States would not lead to revolutionary contagion of the older economies of individual European nations, which were closed systems bounded by their national borders. When the so-called <a href="/wiki/Panic_of_1857" title="Panic of 1857">Panic of 1857</a> in the United States spread globally, it broke all economic theory models, and was the first truly global economic crisis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>Financial necessity had forced Marx to abandon economic studies in 1844 and give thirteen years to working on other projects. He had always sought to return to economics.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_International_and_Das_Kapital">First International and <i>Das Kapital</i></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_Das_Kapital_Marx_1867.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1681" data-file-width="997" decoding="async" height="287" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_Das_Kapital_Marx_1867.jpg/170px-Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_Das_Kapital_Marx_1867.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_Das_Kapital_Marx_1867.jpg/255px-Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_Das_Kapital_Marx_1867.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_Das_Kapital_Marx_1867.jpg/340px-Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_Das_Kapital_Marx_1867.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zentralbibliothek_Z%C3%BCrich_Das_Kapital_Marx_1867.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first volume of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capital:_Critique_of_Political_Economy" title="Capital: Critique of Political Economy">Das Kapital</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>Marx continued to write articles for the <i>New York Daily Tribune</i> as long as he was sure that the <i>Tribune</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s editorial policy was still progressive. However, the departure of Charles Dana from the paper in late 1861 and the resultant change in the editorial board brought about a new editorial policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> No longer was the <i>Tribune</i> to be a strong <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abolitionist" title="Abolitionist">abolitionist</a> paper dedicated to a complete <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a> victory. The new editorial board supported an immediate peace between the Union and the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederacy</a> in the Civil War in the United States with slavery left intact in the Confederacy. Marx strongly disagreed with this new political position and in 1863 was forced to withdraw as a writer for the <i>Tribune</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973345_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973345-155">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1864, Marx became involved in the <a href="/wiki/International_Workingmen%27s_Association" title="International Workingmen's Association">International Workingmen's Association</a> (also known as the First International),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicolaievsky2007_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nicolaievsky2007-121">[121]</a></sup> to whose General Council he was elected at its inception in 1864.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicolaievsky2007-267_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nicolaievsky2007-267-156">[156]</a></sup> In that organisation, Marx was involved in the struggle against the anarchist wing centred on <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Bakunin" title="Mikhail Bakunin">Mikhail Bakunin</a> (1814–1876).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-egsbio_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-egsbio-134">[134]</a></sup> Although Marx won this contest, the transfer of the seat of the General Council from London to New York in 1872, which Marx supported, led to the decline of the International.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JessopWheatley1999-526_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-JessopWheatley1999-526-157">[157]</a></sup> The most important political event during the existence of the International was the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Commune" title="Paris Commune">Paris Commune</a> of 1871 when the citizens of Paris rebelled against their government and held the city for two months. In response to the bloody suppression of this rebellion, Marx wrote one of his most famous pamphlets, "<a href="/wiki/The_Civil_War_in_France" title="The Civil War in France">The Civil War in France</a>", a defence of the Commune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Curtis1997_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Curtis1997-158">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup>
