</p><p>Given the repeated failures and frustrations of workers' revolutions and movements, Marx also sought to understand and provide a critique suitable for the <a href="/wiki/Capitalist_mode_of_production_(Marxist_theory)" title="Capitalist mode of production (Marxist theory)">capitalist mode of production</a>, and hence spent a great deal of time in the reading room of the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a> studying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-20_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-20-160">[160]</a></sup> By 1857, Marx had accumulated over 800 pages of notes and short essays on capital, <a href="/wiki/Landed_property" title="Landed property">landed property</a>, wage labour, the state, and foreign trade, and the world market, though this work did not appear in print until 1939, under the title <i><a href="/wiki/Grundrisse" title="Grundrisse"><i>Outlines of the Critique of Political Economy</i></a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Segrest2002_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Segrest2002-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1859, Marx published <i><a href="/wiki/A_Contribution_to_the_Critique_of_Political_Economy" title="A Contribution to the Critique of Political Economy">A Contribution to the Critique of Political Economy</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> his first serious <a href="/wiki/Critique_of_political_economy" title="Critique of political economy">critique of political economy</a>. This work was intended merely as a preview of his three-volume <i><a href="/wiki/Das_Kapital" title="Das Kapital">Das Kapital</a></i> (English title: <i>Capital: Critique of Political Economy</i>), which he intended to publish at a later date. In <i>A Contribution to the Critique of Political Economy</i>, Marx begun to critically examine axioms and categories of economic thinking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-167">[167]</a></sup> The work was enthusiastically received, and the edition sold out quickly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973318_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFedoseyev1973318-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karl-Marx.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="502" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Karl-Marx.jpg/170px-Karl-Marx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Karl-Marx.jpg/255px-Karl-Marx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Karl-Marx.jpg/340px-Karl-Marx.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karl-Marx.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marx photographed by John Mayall, 1875</div></div></div>
<p>The successful sales of <i><a href="/wiki/A_Contribution_to_the_Critique_of_Political_Economy" title="A Contribution to the Critique of Political Economy">A Contribution to the Critique of Political Economy</a></i> stimulated Marx in the early 1860s to finish work on the three large volumes that would compose his major life's work – <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Das Kapital</i></span> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Theories_of_Surplus_Value" title="Theories of Surplus Value">Theories of Surplus Value</a></i>, which discussed and critiqued the theoreticians of political economy, particularly <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Ricardo" title="David Ricardo">David Ricardo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-egsbio_134-3"><a href="#cite_note-egsbio-134">[134]</a></sup> <i>Theories of Surplus Value</i> is often referred to as the fourth volume of <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Das Kapital</i></span> and constitutes one of the first comprehensive treatises on the <a href="/wiki/History_of_economic_thought" title="History of economic thought">history of economic thought</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rockmore2002_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rockmore2002-169">[169]</a></sup> In 1867, the first volume of <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Das Kapital</i></span> was published, a work which critically analysed capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrewerMarx1984-15_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrewerMarx1984-15-170">[170]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-167">[167]</a></sup> <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Das Kapital</i></span> proposes an explanation of the "laws of motion" of the <a href="/wiki/Mode_of_production" title="Mode of production">mode of production</a> from its origins to its future by describing the dynamics of the accumulation of capital. With topics such as the growth of <a href="/wiki/Wage_labour" title="Wage labour">wage labour</a>, the transformation of the workplace, <a href="/wiki/Capital_accumulation" title="Capital accumulation">capital accumulation</a>, competition, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banking" title="Banking">banking</a> system, the <a href="/wiki/Tendency_of_the_rate_of_profit_to_fall" title="Tendency of the rate of profit to fall">tendency of the rate of profit to fall</a>, land-rents. As well as how waged labour continually reproduce the rule of capital, <i>et cetera</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-172">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-173">[173]</a></sup> Marx proposes that the driving force of capital is in the <a href="/wiki/Exploitation_of_labour" title="Exploitation of labour">exploitation</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labour_(economics)" title="Labour (economics)">labor</a>, whose unpaid work is the ultimate source of <a href="/wiki/Surplus_value" title="Surplus value">surplus value</a>.
</p><p>Demand for a Russian language edition of <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Das Kapital</i></span> soon led to the printing of 3,000 copies of the book in the Russian language, which was published on 27 March 1872. By the autumn of 1871, the entire first edition of the German-language edition of <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Das Kapital</i></span> had been sold out and a second edition was published.
</p><p>Volumes II and III of <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Das Kapital</i></span> remained mere manuscripts upon which Marx continued to work for the rest of his life. Both volumes were published by Engels after Marx's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-egsbio_134-4"><a href="#cite_note-egsbio-134">[134]</a></sup> Volume II of <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Das Kapital</i></span> was prepared and published by Engels in July 1893 under the name <i>Capital II: The Process of Circulation of Capital</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Volume III of <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Das Kapital</i></span> was published a year later in October 1894 under the name <i>Capital III: The Process of Capitalist Production as a Whole</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> <i>Theories of Surplus Value</i> derived from the sprawling <i>Economic Manuscripts of 1861–1863</i>, a <i>second</i> draft for <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Das Kapital</i></span>, the latter spanning volumes 30–34 of the <i>Collected Works of Marx and Engels</i>. Specifically, <i>Theories of Surplus Value</i> runs from the latter part of the <i>Collected Works' </i> thirtieth volume through the end of their thirty-second volume;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup> meanwhile, the larger <i>Economic Manuscripts of 1861–1863</i> run from the start of the <i>Collected Works' </i> thirtieth volume through the first half of their thirty-fourth volume. The latter half of the Collected Works' thirty-fourth volume consists of the surviving fragments of the <i>Economic Manuscripts of 1863–1864</i>, which represented a <i>third</i> draft for <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Das Kapital</i></span>, and a large portion of which is included as an appendix to the Penguin edition of <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Das Kapital</i></span>, volume I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> A German-language abridged edition of <i>Theories of Surplus Value</i> was published in 1905 and in 1910. This abridged edition was translated into English and published in 1951 in London, but the complete unabridged edition of <i>Theories of Surplus Value</i> was published as the "fourth volume" of <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Das Kapital</i></span> in 1963 and 1971 in Moscow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marx_old.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2646" data-file-width="1864" decoding="async" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Marx_old.jpg/170px-Marx_old.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Marx_old.jpg/255px-Marx_old.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Marx_old.jpg/340px-Marx_old.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marx_old.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marx in 1882</div></div></div>
<p>During the last decade of his life, Marx's health declined and he became incapable of the sustained effort that had characterised his previous work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-egsbio_134-5"><a href="#cite_note-egsbio-134">[134]</a></sup> He did manage to comment substantially on contemporary politics, particularly in Germany and Russia. His <i><a href="/wiki/Critique_of_the_Gotha_Programme" title="Critique of the Gotha Programme">Critique of the Gotha Programme</a></i> opposed the tendency of his followers <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Liebknecht" title="Wilhelm Liebknecht">Wilhelm Liebknecht</a> and <a href="/wiki/August_Bebel" title="August Bebel">August Bebel</a> to compromise with the <a href="/wiki/State_socialism" title="State socialism">state socialism</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Lassalle" title="Ferdinand Lassalle">Ferdinand Lassalle</a> in the interests of a united socialist party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-egsbio_134-6"><a href="#cite_note-egsbio-134">[134]</a></sup> This work is also notable for another famous Marx quote: "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/From_each_according_to_his_ability,_to_each_according_to_his_need" title="From each according to his ability, to each according to his need">From each according to his ability, to each according to his need</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CGP_P1_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-CGP_P1-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a letter to <a href="/wiki/Vera_Zasulich" title="Vera Zasulich">Vera Zasulich</a> dated 8 March 1881, Marx contemplated the possibility of Russia's bypassing the capitalist stage of development and building communism on the basis of the common ownership of land characteristic of the village <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mir_(social)" title="Mir (social)">mir</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-egsbio_134-7"><a href="#cite_note-egsbio-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> While admitting that Russia's rural "commune is the fulcrum of social regeneration in Russia", Marx also warned that in order for the mir to operate as a means for moving straight to the socialist stage without a preceding capitalist stage it "would first be necessary to eliminate the deleterious influences which are assailing it [the rural commune] from all sides".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k72_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-k72-183">[183]</a></sup> Given the elimination of these pernicious influences, Marx allowed that "normal conditions of spontaneous development" of the rural commune could exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k72_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-k72-183">[183]</a></sup> However, in the same letter to Vera Zasulich he points out that "at the core of the capitalist system ... lies the complete separation of the producer from the means of production".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-k72_183-2"><a href="#cite_note-k72-183">[183]</a></sup> In one of the drafts of this letter, Marx reveals his growing passion for anthropology, motivated by his belief that future communism would be a return on a higher level to the communism of our prehistoric past. He wrote that "the historical trend of our age is the fatal crisis which capitalist production has undergone in the European and American countries where it has reached its highest peak, a crisis that will end in its destruction, in the return of modern society to a higher form of the most archaic type – collective production and appropriation". He added that "the vitality of primitive communities was incomparably greater than that of Semitic, Greek, Roman, etc. societies, and, a fortiori, that of modern capitalist societies".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup> Before he died, Marx asked Engels to write up these ideas, which were published in 1884 under the title <i><a href="/wiki/The_Origin_of_the_Family,_Private_Property_and_the_State" title="The Origin of the Family, Private Property and the State">The Origin of the Family, Private Property and the State</a></i>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jenny_laura_marx.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="494" data-file-width="358" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Jenny_laura_marx.jpg/170px-Jenny_laura_marx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Jenny_laura_marx.jpg/255px-Jenny_laura_marx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Jenny_laura_marx.jpg/340px-Jenny_laura_marx.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jenny_laura_marx.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Jenny Carolina and Jenny Laura Marx (1869): all the Marx daughters were named Jenny in honour of their mother, Jenny von Westphalen.</div></div></div>
<p>Marx and von Westphalen had seven children together, but partly owing to the poor conditions in which they lived whilst in London, only three survived to adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> Their children were: <a href="/wiki/Jenny_Longuet" title="Jenny Longuet">Jenny Caroline</a> (m. Longuet; 1844–1883); <a href="/wiki/Laura_Marx" title="Laura Marx">Jenny Laura</a> (m. Lafargue; 1845–1911); Edgar (1847–1855); Henry Edward Guy ("Guido"; 1849–1850); Jenny Eveline Frances ("Franziska"; 1851–1852); <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Marx" title="Eleanor Marx">Jenny Julia Eleanor</a> (1855–1898) and one more who died before being named (July 1857). According to his son-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Lafargue" title="Paul Lafargue">Paul Lafargue</a>, Marx was a loving father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> In 1962, there were allegations that Marx fathered a son, Freddy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montefiore_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Montefiore-187">[187]</a></sup> out of wedlock by his housekeeper, <a href="/wiki/Helene_Demuth" title="Helene Demuth">Helene Demuth</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karl_Marx_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karl_Marx-188">[188]</a></sup> but the claim is disputed for lack of documented evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carver,_1991_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carver,_1991-189">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marx frequently used pseudonyms, often when renting a house or flat, apparently to make it harder for the authorities to track him down. While in Paris, he used that of "Monsieur Ramboz", whilst in London, he signed off his letters as "A. Williams". His friends referred to him as "Moor", owing to his dark complexion and black curly hair, while he encouraged his children to call him "Old Nick" and "Charley".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheen_2001._p._152_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wheen_2001._p._152-190">[190]</a></sup> He also bestowed nicknames and pseudonyms on his friends and family as well, referring to Friedrich Engels as "General", his housekeeper Helene as "Lenchen" or "Nym", while one of his daughters, Jennychen, was referred to as "Qui Qui, <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">Emperor of China</a>" and another, Laura, was known as "<a href="/wiki/Cockatoo" title="Cockatoo">Kakadou</a>" or "the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khoikhoi" title="Khoikhoi">Hottentot</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheen_2001._p._152_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wheen_2001._p._152-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health">Health</span></h3>
<p>Marx drank heavily until his death after joining the Trier Tavern Club drinking society in the 1830s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drinking1_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-drinking1-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marx was afflicted by poor health (what he himself described as "the wretchedness of existence")<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> and various authors have sought to describe and explain it. His biographer Werner Blumenberg attributed it to liver and gall problems which Marx had in 1849 and from which he was never afterward free, exacerbated by an unsuitable lifestyle. The attacks often came with headaches, eye inflammation, neuralgia in the head, and rheumatic pains. A serious nervous disorder appeared in 1877 and protracted insomnia was a consequence, which Marx fought with narcotics. The illness was aggravated by excessive nocturnal work and faulty diet. Marx was fond of highly seasoned dishes, smoked fish, caviare, pickled cucumbers, "none of which are good for liver patients", but he also liked wine and liqueurs and smoked an enormous amount "and since he had no money, it was usually bad-quality cigars". From 1863, Marx complained a lot about boils: "These are very frequent with liver patients and may be due to the same causes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> The abscesses were so bad that Marx could neither sit nor work upright. According to Blumenberg, Marx's irritability is often found in liver patients:
</p>
<blockquote><p>The illness emphasised certain traits in his character. He argued cuttingly, his biting satire did not shrink at insults, and his expressions could be rude and cruel. Though in general Marx had blind faith in his closest friends, nevertheless he himself complained that he was sometimes too mistrustful and unjust even to them. His verdicts, not only about enemies but even about friends, were sometimes so harsh that even less sensitive people would take offence ... There must have been few whom he did not criticize like this ... not even Engels was an exception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>According to Princeton historian <a href="/wiki/Jerrold_Seigel" title="Jerrold Seigel">Jerrold Seigel</a>, in his late teens, Marx may have had pneumonia or pleurisy, the effects of which led to his being exempted from Prussian military service. In later life whilst working on <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Das Kapital</i></span> (which he never completed),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> Marx suffered from a trio of afflictions. A liver ailment, probably hereditary, was aggravated by overwork, a bad diet, and lack of sleep. Inflammation of the eyes was induced by too much work at night. A third affliction, eruption of carbuncles or boils, "was probably brought on by general physical debility to which the various features of Marx's style of life – alcohol, tobacco, poor diet, and failure to sleep – all contributed. Engels often exhorted Marx to alter this dangerous regime". In Seigel's thesis, what lay behind this punishing sacrifice of his health may have been guilt about self-involvement and egoism, originally induced in Karl Marx by his father.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2007, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retrodiagnosis" title="Retrodiagnosis">retrodiagnosis</a> of Marx's skin disease was made by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dermatologist" title="Dermatologist">dermatologist</a> Sam Shuster of <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_University" title="Newcastle University">Newcastle University</a> and for Shuster, the most probable explanation was that Marx suffered not from liver problems, but from <a href="/wiki/Hidradenitis_suppurativa" title="Hidradenitis suppurativa">hidradenitis suppurativa</a>, a recurring infective condition arising from blockage of <a href="/wiki/Apocrine" title="Apocrine">apocrine</a> ducts opening into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hair_follicles" title="Hair follicles">hair follicles</a>. This condition, which was not described in the English medical literature until 1933 (hence would not have been known to Marx's physicians), can produce joint pain (which could be misdiagnosed as rheumatic disorder) and painful eye conditions. To arrive at his retrodiagnosis, Shuster considered the primary material: the Marx correspondence published in the 50 volumes of the <i>Marx/Engels Collected Works</i>. There, "although the skin lesions were called 'furuncles', 'boils' and 'carbuncles' by Marx, his wife, and his physicians, they were too persistent, recurrent, destructive and site-specific for that diagnosis". The sites of the persistent 'carbuncles' were noted repeatedly in the armpits, groins, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perianal" title="Perianal">perianal</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genital" title="Genital">genital</a> (<a href="/wiki/Human_penis" title="Human penis">penis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scrotum" title="Scrotum">scrotum</a>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suprapubic" title="Suprapubic">suprapubic</a> regions and inner thighs, "favoured sites of hidradenitis suppurativa". Professor Shuster claimed the diagnosis "can now be made definitively".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>Shuster went on to consider the potential <a href="/wiki/Psychosocial" title="Psychosocial">psychosocial</a> effects of the disease, noting that the skin is an organ of communication and that hidradenitis suppurativa produces much psychological distress, including loathing and disgust and depression of self-image, mood, and well-being, feelings for which Shuster found "much evidence" in the Marx correspondence. Professor Shuster went on to ask himself whether the mental effects of the disease affected Marx's work and even helped him to develop <a href="/wiki/Marx%27s_theory_of_alienation" title="Marx's theory of alienation">his theory of alienation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Grave_of_Karl_Marx_Highgate_Cemetery_in_London_2016_(10).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4320" decoding="async" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Grave_of_Karl_Marx_Highgate_Cemetery_in_London_2016_%2810%29.jpg/250px-Grave_of_Karl_Marx_Highgate_Cemetery_in_London_2016_%2810%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Grave_of_Karl_Marx_Highgate_Cemetery_in_London_2016_%2810%29.jpg/375px-Grave_of_Karl_Marx_Highgate_Cemetery_in_London_2016_%2810%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Grave_of_Karl_Marx_Highgate_Cemetery_in_London_2016_%2810%29.jpg/500px-Grave_of_Karl_Marx_Highgate_Cemetery_in_London_2016_%2810%29.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Grave_of_Karl_Marx_Highgate_Cemetery_in_London_2016_(10).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Karl_Marx" title="Tomb of Karl Marx">Tomb of Karl Marx</a>, East <a href="/wiki/Highgate_Cemetery" title="Highgate Cemetery">Highgate Cemetery</a>, London</div></div></div>
<p>Following the death of his wife Jenny in December 1881, Marx developed a <a href="/wiki/Catarrh" title="Catarrh">catarrh</a> that kept him in ill health for the last 15 months of his life. It eventually brought on the <a href="/wiki/Bronchitis" title="Bronchitis">bronchitis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pleurisy" title="Pleurisy">pleurisy</a> that killed him in London on 14 March 1883, when he died a <a href="/wiki/Statelessness" title="Statelessness">stateless person</a> at age 64.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stateless_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-stateless-198">[198]</a></sup> Family and friends in London buried his body in <a href="/wiki/Highgate_Cemetery" title="Highgate Cemetery">Highgate Cemetery</a> (East), London, on 17 March 1883 in an area reserved for agnostics and atheists (<a href="/wiki/George_Eliot" title="George Eliot">George Eliot</a>'s grave is nearby). According to <a href="/wiki/Francis_Wheen" title="Francis Wheen">Francis Wheen</a> there were between nine and eleven mourners at his funeral,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GouldMcGarr2007_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-GouldMcGarr2007-200">[200]</a></sup> however research from contemporary sources identifies thirteen named individuals attending the funeral. They were, <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Marx" title="Eleanor Marx">Eleanor Marx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edward_Aveling" title="Edward Aveling">Edward Aveling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_Lafargue" title="Paul Lafargue">Paul Lafargue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Longuet" title="Charles Longuet">Charles Longuet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helene_Demuth" title="Helene Demuth">Helene Demuth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Liebknecht" title="Wilhelm Liebknecht">Wilhelm Liebknecht</a>, Gottlieb Lemke, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Lessner" title="Frederick Lessner">Frederick Lessner</a>, G Lochner, Sir <a href="/wiki/Ray_Lankester" title="Ray Lankester">Ray Lankester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Schorlemmer" title="Carl Schorlemmer">Carl Schorlemmer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Radford" title="Ernest Radford">Ernest Radford</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> A contemporary newspaper account claims that 25 to 30 relatives and friends attended the funeral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> A writer in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Graphic" title="The Graphic">The Graphic</a></i> noted that 'By a strange blunder ... his death was not announced for two days, and then as having taken place at Paris. The next day the correction came from Paris; and when his friends and followers hastened to his house in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haverstock_Hill" title="Haverstock Hill">Haverstock Hill</a>, to learn the time and place of burial, they learned that he was already in the cold ground. But for this secresy [sic] and haste, a great popular demonstration would undoubtedly have been held over his grave'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>
Several of his closest friends spoke at his funeral, including <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Liebknecht" title="Wilhelm Liebknecht">Wilhelm Liebknecht</a> and Friedrich Engels. Engels' speech included the passage: <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>On the 14th of March, at a quarter to three in the afternoon, the greatest living thinker ceased to think. He had been left alone for scarcely two minutes, and when we came back we found him in his armchair, peacefully gone to sleep – but forever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1883:_The_death_of_Karl_Marx_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-1883:_The_death_of_Karl_Marx-204">[204]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Marx's surviving daughters <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Marx" title="Eleanor Marx">Eleanor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laura_Marx" title="Laura Marx">Laura</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Charles_Longuet" title="Charles Longuet">Charles Longuet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Lafargue" title="Paul Lafargue">Paul Lafargue</a>, Marx's two French socialist sons-in-law, were also in attendance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GouldMcGarr2007_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-GouldMcGarr2007-200">[200]</a></sup> He had been predeceased by his wife and his eldest daughter, the latter dying a few months earlier in January 1883. Liebknecht, a founder and leader of the German Social Democratic Party, gave a speech in German and Longuet, a prominent figure in the French working-class movement, made a short statement in French.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GouldMcGarr2007_200-2"><a href="#cite_note-GouldMcGarr2007-200">[200]</a></sup> Two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telegram" title="Telegram">telegrams</a> from workers' parties in France and Spain<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (April 2021)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> were also read out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GouldMcGarr2007_200-3"><a href="#cite_note-GouldMcGarr2007-200">[200]</a></sup> Together with Engels's speech, this constituted the entire programme of the funeral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GouldMcGarr2007_200-4"><a href="#cite_note-GouldMcGarr2007-200">[200]</a></sup> Non-relatives attending the funeral included three communist associates of Marx: Friedrich Lessner, imprisoned for three years after the <a href="/wiki/Cologne_Communist_Trial" title="Cologne Communist Trial">Cologne Communist Trial</a> of 1852; G. Lochner, whom Engels described as "an old member of the Communist League"; and <a href="/wiki/Carl_Schorlemmer" title="Carl Schorlemmer">Carl Schorlemmer</a>, a professor of chemistry in Manchester, a member of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> and a communist activist involved in the 1848 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revolution_in_Baden" title="Revolution in Baden">Baden revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GouldMcGarr2007_200-5"><a href="#cite_note-GouldMcGarr2007-200">[200]</a></sup> Another attendee of the funeral was <a href="/wiki/Ray_Lankester" title="Ray Lankester">Ray Lankester</a>, a British zoologist who would later become a prominent academic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GouldMcGarr2007_200-6"><a href="#cite_note-GouldMcGarr2007-200">[200]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marx left a personal estate valued for probate at £250 (equivalent to £26,788 in 2021<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-UK_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-UK-205">[205]</a></sup>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-probate_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-probate-206">[206]</a></sup> Upon his own death in 1895, Engels left Marx's two surviving daughters a "significant portion" of his considerable estate (valued in 2011 at US$4.8 million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Montefiore_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-Montefiore-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marx and his family were reburied on a new site nearby in November 1954. The <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Karl_Marx" title="Tomb of Karl Marx">tomb</a> at the new site, unveiled on 14 March 1956,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup> bears the carved message: "<a href="/wiki/Workers_of_the_world,_unite!" title="Workers of the world, unite!">Workers of All Lands Unite</a>", the final line of <i>The Communist Manifesto</i>; and, from the 11th "<a href="/wiki/Theses_on_Feuerbach" title="Theses on Feuerbach">Thesis on Feuerbach</a>" (as edited by Engels), "The philosophers have only interpreted the world in various ways—the point however is to change it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wh1_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-wh1-208">[208]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_Great_Britain" title="Communist Party of Great Britain">Communist Party of Great Britain (CPGB)</a> had the monument with a portrait bust by <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Bradshaw" title="Laurence Bradshaw">Laurence Bradshaw</a> erected and Marx's original tomb had only humble adornment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wh1_208-1"><a href="#cite_note-wh1-208">[208]</a></sup> Black civil rights leader and CPGB activist <a href="/wiki/Claudia_Jones" title="Claudia Jones">Claudia Jones</a> was later buried beside Karl Marx's tomb.
</p><p>The Marxist historian <a href="/wiki/Eric_Hobsbawm" title="Eric Hobsbawm">Eric Hobsbawm</a> remarked: "One cannot say Marx died a failure" because although he had not achieved a large following of disciples in Britain, his writings had already begun to make an impact on the left-wing movements in Germany and Russia. Within 25 years of his death, the continental European socialist parties that acknowledged Marx's influence on their politics were each gaining between 15 and 47 percent in those countries with <a href="/wiki/Representative_democracy" title="Representative democracy">representative democratic</a> elections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup>
</p>

<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background:transparent;border-top:1px solid #aaa;"><div class="sidebar-list-title-c"><a href="/wiki/Marxist_schools_of_thought" title="Marxist schools of thought">Other variants</a></div></div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content"><div class="hlist hlist-separated">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Influences_on_Karl_Marx" title="Influences on Karl Marx">Influences on Karl Marx</a></div>
<p>Marx's thought demonstrates influences from many thinkers including, but not limited to:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel</a>'s philosophy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-20-23_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-20-23-210">[210]</a></sup></li>
<li>The classical <a href="/wiki/Political_economy" title="Political economy">political economy</a> (economics) of <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Ricardo" title="David Ricardo">David Ricardo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherman1995_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sherman1995-211">[211]</a></sup> as well as <a href="/wiki/Jean_Charles_L%C3%A9onard_de_Sismondi" title="Jean Charles Léonard de Sismondi">Jean Charles Léonard de Sismondi</a>'s critique of laissez-faire economics and analysis of the precarious state of the proletariat<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paresh_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paresh-212">[212]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Left_in_France" title="History of the Left in France">French socialist thought</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherman1995_211-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sherman1995-211">[211]</a></sup> in particular the thought of <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henri_de_Saint-Simon" title="Henri de Saint-Simon">Henri de Saint-Simon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Joseph_Proudhon" title="Pierre-Joseph Proudhon">Pierre-Joseph Proudhon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Fourier" title="Charles Fourier">Charles Fourier</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beilharz1992_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beilharz1992-213">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark1998_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clark1998-214">[214]</a></sup></li>
<li>Earlier German philosophical materialism among the <a href="/wiki/Young_Hegelians" title="Young Hegelians">Young Hegelians</a>, particularly that of <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Feuerbach" title="Ludwig Feuerbach">Ludwig Feuerbach</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Bauer" title="Bruno Bauer">Bruno Bauer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-et_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-et-68">[68]</a></sup> as well as the French materialism of the late 18th century, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diderot" title="Diderot">Diderot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claude_Adrien_Helv%C3%A9tius" title="Claude Adrien Helvétius">Claude Adrien Helvétius</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D%27Holbach" title="D'Holbach">d'Holbach</a></li>
<li>The working class analysis by <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bottomore1991_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bottomore1991-64">[64]</a></sup> as well as the early descriptions of class provided by French liberals and Saint-Simonians such as <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Guizot" title="François Guizot">François Guizot</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustin_Thierry" title="Augustin Thierry">Augustin Thierry</a></li>
<li>Marx's Judaic legacy has been identified as formative to both his moral outlook<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup> and his materialist philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Marx's view of history, which came to be called <a href="/wiki/Historical_materialism" title="Historical materialism">historical materialism</a> (controversially adapted as the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Dialectical_materialism" title="Dialectical materialism">dialectical materialism</a> by Engels and Lenin), certainly shows the influence of Hegel's claim that one should view reality (and history) <a href="/wiki/Dialectic" title="Dialectic">dialectically</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-20-23_210-1"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-20-23-210">[210]</a></sup> However, Hegel had thought in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Idealist" title="Idealist">idealist</a> terms, putting ideas in the forefront, whereas Marx sought to rewrite dialectics in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Materialist" title="Materialist">materialist</a> terms, arguing for the primacy of matter over idea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_78-5"><a href="#cite_note-sep-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-20-23_210-2"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-20-23-210">[210]</a></sup> Where Hegel saw the "spirit" as driving history, Marx saw this as an unnecessary mystification, obscuring the reality of humanity and its physical actions shaping the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-20-23_210-3"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-20-23-210">[210]</a></sup> He wrote that Hegelianism stood the movement of reality on its head, and that one needed to set it upon its feet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-20-23_210-4"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-20-23-210">[210]</a></sup> Despite his dislike of mystical terms, Marx used <a href="/wiki/Gothic_fiction" title="Gothic fiction">Gothic</a> language in several of his works: in <i>The Communist Manifesto</i> he proclaims "A spectre is haunting Europe – the spectre of communism. All the powers of old Europe have entered into a holy alliance to exorcise this spectre", and in <i>The Capital</i> he refers to capital as "<a href="/wiki/Necromancy" title="Necromancy">necromancy</a> that surrounds the products of labour".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-217">[217]</a></sup>
</p><p>Though inspired by French socialist and sociological thought,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherman1995_211-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sherman1995-211">[211]</a></sup> Marx criticised <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utopian_socialists" title="Utopian socialists">utopian socialists</a>, arguing that their favoured small-scale socialistic communities would be bound to marginalisation and poverty and that only a large-scale change in the <a href="/wiki/Economic_system" title="Economic system">economic system</a> can bring about real change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark1998_214-1"><a href="#cite_note-Clark1998-214">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>The other important contributions to Marx's revision of Hegelianism came from Engels's book, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Condition_of_the_Working_Class_in_England_in_1844" title="The Condition of the Working Class in England in 1844">The Condition of the Working Class in England in 1844</a></i>, which led Marx to conceive of the historical dialectic in terms of <a href="/wiki/Class_conflict" title="Class conflict">class conflict</a> and to see the modern working class as the most progressive force for revolution,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bottomore1991_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bottomore1991-64">[64]</a></sup> as well as from the social democrat <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Wilhelm_Schulz" title="Friedrich Wilhelm Schulz">Friedrich Wilhelm Schulz</a>, who in <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Die Bewegung der Produktion</i></span> described the movement of society as "flowing from the contradiction between the forces of production and the mode of production."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levine_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levine-218">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated144_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated144-219">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marx believed that he could study history and society scientifically and discern tendencies of history and the resulting outcome of social conflicts. Some followers of Marx, therefore, concluded that a communist revolution would inevitably occur. However, Marx famously asserted in the eleventh of his "<a href="/wiki/Theses_on_Feuerbach" title="Theses on Feuerbach">Theses on Feuerbach</a>" that "philosophers have only interpreted the world, in various ways; the point however is to change it" and he clearly dedicated himself to trying to alter the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-23-24_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-23-24-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wh1_208-2"><a href="#cite_note-wh1-208">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marx's theories inspired several theories and disciplines of future including, but not limited to:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Critique_of_political_economy" title="Critique of political economy">Contemporary critique of political economy</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kondratiev_wave" title="Kondratiev wave">Kondratiev wave</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kuznets_swing" title="Kuznets swing">Kuznets swing</a></li>
<li>Theory of <a href="/wiki/Underconsumption" title="Underconsumption">Underconsumption</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Creative_destruction" title="Creative destruction">Creative destruction</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Crisis_theory" title="Crisis theory">Crisis theory</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantitative_Economic_History" title="Quantitative Economic History">Quantitative Economic History</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/World-systems_theory" title="World-systems theory">World-systems theory</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy_and_social_thought">Philosophy and social thought</span></h3>
<p>Marx's polemic with other thinkers often occurred through <a href="/wiki/Critique" title="Critique">critique</a> and thus he has been called "the first great user of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_method" title="Critical method">critical method</a> in social sciences".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-20-23_210-5"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-20-23-210">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherman1995_211-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sherman1995-211">[211]</a></sup> He criticised speculative philosophy, equating <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a> with ideology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bannerji2001_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bannerji2001-220">[220]</a></sup> By adopting this approach, Marx attempted to separate key findings from ideological biases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherman1995_211-4"><a href="#cite_note-Sherman1995-211">[211]</a></sup> This set him apart from many contemporary philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-23-24_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-23-24-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_nature">Human nature</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Marx%27s_theory_of_human_nature" title="Marx's theory of human nature">Marx's theory of human nature</a></div>

.theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:144px;max-width:144px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:162px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:G.W.F._Hegel_(by_Sichling,_after_Sebbers).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="573" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/G.W.F._Hegel_%28by_Sichling%2C_after_Sebbers%29.jpg/142px-G.W.F._Hegel_%28by_Sichling%2C_after_Sebbers%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/G.W.F._Hegel_%28by_Sichling%2C_after_Sebbers%29.jpg/213px-G.W.F._Hegel_%28by_Sichling%2C_after_Sebbers%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/G.W.F._Hegel_%28by_Sichling%2C_after_Sebbers%29.jpg/284px-G.W.F._Hegel_%28by_Sichling%2C_after_Sebbers%29.jpg 2x" width="142"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:144px;max-width:144px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:162px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2063" data-file-width="1807" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg/142px-Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg/213px-Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg/284px-Ludwig_Andreas_Feuerbach.jpg 2x" width="142"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">The philosophers <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">G.W.F. Hegel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Feuerbach" title="Ludwig Feuerbach">Ludwig Feuerbach</a>, whose ideas on dialectics heavily influenced Marx</div></div></div></div>
<p>Like Tocqueville, who described a faceless and bureaucratic <a href="/wiki/Despotism" title="Despotism">despotism</a> with no identifiable despot,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> Marx also broke with classical thinkers who spoke of a single tyrant and with <a href="/wiki/Montesquieu" title="Montesquieu">Montesquieu</a>, who discussed the nature of the single despot. Instead, Marx set out to analyse "the despotism of capital".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> Fundamentally, Marx assumed that <a href="/wiki/Human_history" title="Human history">human history</a> involves transforming <a href="/wiki/Human_nature" title="Human nature">human nature</a>, which encompasses both human beings and material objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ollman1973_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ollman1973-223">[223]</a></sup> Humans recognise that they possess both actual and potential selves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marx_labour_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marx_labour-224">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marx_critique_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marx_critique-225">[225]</a></sup> For both Marx and Hegel, self-development begins with an experience of internal <a href="/wiki/Marx%27s_theory_of_alienation" title="Marx's theory of alienation">alienation</a> stemming from this recognition, followed by a realisation that the actual self, as a <a href="/wiki/Subject_(philosophy)" title="Subject (philosophy)">subjective</a> agent, renders its potential counterpart an <a href="/wiki/Object_(philosophy)" title="Object (philosophy)">object</a> to be apprehended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marx_critique_225-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marx_critique-225">[225]</a></sup> Marx further argues that by moulding nature<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lefever_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lefever-226">[226]</a></sup> in desired ways<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holland_desire_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holland_desire-227">[227]</a></sup> the subject takes the object as its own and thus permits the individual to be actualised as fully human. For Marx, the <a href="/wiki/Marx%27s_theory_of_human_nature" title="Marx's theory of human nature">human nature</a> – <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Gattungswesen</i></span>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Species-being" title="Species-being">species-being</a> – exists as a function of human labour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marx_labour_224-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marx_labour-224">[224]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marx_critique_225-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marx_critique-225">[225]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holland_desire_227-1"><a href="#cite_note-Holland_desire-227">[227]</a></sup> Fundamental to Marx's idea of meaningful labour is the proposition that for a subject to come to terms with its alienated object it must first exert influence upon literal, material objects in the subject's world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marx_objects_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marx_objects-228">[228]</a></sup> Marx acknowledges that Hegel "grasps the nature of work and comprehends objective man, authentic because actual, as the result of his <em>own work</em>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marx_work_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marx_work-229">[229]</a></sup> but characterises Hegelian self-development as unduly "spiritual" and abstract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marx_spiritual_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marx_spiritual-230">[230]</a></sup> Marx thus departs from Hegel by insisting that "the fact that man is a corporeal, actual, sentient, objective being with natural capacities means that he has actual, sensuous objects for his nature as objects of his life-expression, or that he can only express his life in actual sensuous objects".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marx_objects_228-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marx_objects-228">[228]</a></sup> Consequently, Marx revises Hegelian "work" into material "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labour_(economics)" title="Labour (economics)">labour</a>" and in the context of human capacity to transform nature the term "<a href="/wiki/Labour_power" title="Labour power">labour power</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_78-6"><a href="#cite_note-sep-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Labour.2C_class_struggle_and_false_consciousness"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Labour,_class_struggle_and_false_consciousness">Labour, class struggle and false consciousness</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alienation_(Marxism)" title="Alienation (Marxism)">Alienation (Marxism)</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Class_struggle" title="Class struggle">Class struggle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Capitalist_mode_of_production_(Marxist_theory)" title="Capitalist mode of production (Marxist theory)">Capitalist mode of production (Marxist theory)</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The history of all hitherto existing society is the history of class struggles.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Karl Marx, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Communist_Manifesto" title="The Communist Manifesto">The Communist Manifesto</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarxEngels2009pp5_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-MarxEngels2009pp5-231">[231]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marx_et_Engels_%C3%A0_Shanghai.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1449" data-file-width="897" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Marx_et_Engels_%C3%A0_Shanghai.jpg/170px-Marx_et_Engels_%C3%A0_Shanghai.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Marx_et_Engels_%C3%A0_Shanghai.jpg/255px-Marx_et_Engels_%C3%A0_Shanghai.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Marx_et_Engels_%C3%A0_Shanghai.jpg/340px-Marx_et_Engels_%C3%A0_Shanghai.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marx_et_Engels_%C3%A0_Shanghai.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A monument dedicated to Marx and Engels in Shanghai, China</div></div></div>
<p>Marx had a special concern with how people relate to their own labour power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-22_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-22-232">[232]</a></sup> He wrote extensively about this in terms of the problem of <a href="/wiki/Marx%27s_theory_of_alienation" title="Marx's theory of alienation">alienation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mészáros2006_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mészáros2006-233">[233]</a></sup> As with the dialectic, Marx began with a Hegelian notion of alienation but developed a more materialist conception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-22_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-22-232">[232]</a></sup> Capitalism mediates social relationships of production (such as among workers or between workers and capitalists) through commodities, including labour, that are bought and sold on the market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-22_232-2"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-22-232">[232]</a></sup> For Marx, the possibility that one may give up ownership of one's own labour – one's capacity to transform the world – is tantamount to being alienated from one's own nature and it is a spiritual loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-22_232-3"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-22-232">[232]</a></sup> Marx described this loss as <a href="/wiki/Commodity_fetishism" title="Commodity fetishism">commodity fetishism</a>, in which the things that people produce, commodities, appear to have a life and movement of their own to which humans and their behaviour merely adapt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balibar1995_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-Balibar1995-234">[234]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Commodity_fetishism" title="Commodity fetishism">Commodity fetishism</a> provides an example of what Engels called "<a href="/wiki/False_consciousness" title="False consciousness">false consciousness</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KołakowskiFalla2005_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-KołakowskiFalla2005-235">[235]</a></sup> which relates closely to the understanding of ideology. By "ideology", Marx and Engels meant ideas that reflect the interests of a particular class at a particular time in history, but which contemporaries see as universal and eternal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hernadi1989_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hernadi1989-236">[236]</a></sup> Marx and Engels's point was not only that such beliefs are at best half-truths, as they serve an important political function. Put another way, the control that one class exercises over the means of production include not only the production of food or manufactured goods but also the production of ideas (this provides one possible explanation for why members of a subordinate class may hold ideas contrary to their own interests).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_78-7"><a href="#cite_note-sep-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson1990_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson1990-237">[237]</a></sup> An example of this sort of analysis is Marx's understanding of religion, summed up in a passage from the preface<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup> to his 1843 <i>Contribution to the <a href="/wiki/Critique_of_Hegel%27s_Philosophy_of_Right" title="Critique of Hegel's Philosophy of Right">Critique of Hegel's Philosophy of Right</a></i>: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Religious suffering is, at one and the same time, the expression of real suffering and a protest against real suffering. Religion is the sigh of the oppressed creature, the heart of a heartless world, and the soul of soulless conditions. It is the opium of the people. The abolition of religion as the illusory happiness of the people is the demand for their real happiness. To call on them to give up their illusions about their condition is to call on them to give up a condition that requires illusions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MarxO'Malley1977_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-MarxO'Malley1977-239">[239]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Whereas his <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">Gymnasium</a> senior thesis at the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Friedrich-Wilhelm-Gymnasium_(Trier)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Friedrich-Wilhelm-Gymnasium (Trier) (page does not exist)">Gymnasium zu Trier</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich-Wilhelm-Gymnasium_(Trier)" title="de:Friedrich-Wilhelm-Gymnasium (Trier)">de</a>]</span> argued that religion had as its primary social aim the promotion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solidarity_(sociology)" title="Solidarity (sociology)">solidarity</a>, here Marx sees the social function of religion in terms of highlighting/preserving political and economic <i>status quo</i> and <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">inequality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SwatosKivisto1998_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-SwatosKivisto1998-240">[240]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marx was an outspoken opponent of <a href="/wiki/Child_labour" title="Child labour">child labour</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> saying that British industries "could but live by sucking blood, and children's blood too", and that U.S. capital was financed by the "capitalized blood of children".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_217-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-217">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Critique_of_political_economy.2C_history_and_society"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Critique_of_political_economy,_history_and_society">Critique of political economy, history and society</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Critique_of_political_economy" title="Critique of political economy">Critique of political economy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marxian_economics" title="Marxian economics">Marxian economics</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:25em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>But you Communists would introduce community of women, screams the whole bourgeoisie in chorus.
The bourgeois sees in his wife a mere instrument of production. He hears that the means of production are to be exploited in common, and, naturally, can come to no other conclusion than that the lot of being common to all will likewise fall to the women. He has not even a suspicion that the real point aimed at is to do away with the status of women as mere mean of production.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style="">— Karl Marx, <i>The Communist Manifesto</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>Marx's thoughts on labour and its function in reproducing capital were related to the primacy he gave to social relations in determining the society's past, present and future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-20-23_210-6"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-20-23-210">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner2005_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turner2005-244">[244]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-245">[245]</a></sup> (Critics have called this <a href="/wiki/Economic_determinism" title="Economic determinism">economic determinism</a>.) Labour is the precondition for the existence of, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accumulation_of_capital" title="Accumulation of capital">accumulation of capital</a>, which both shape the <a href="/wiki/Social_system" title="Social system">social system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_245-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-245">[245]</a></sup> For Marx, <a href="/wiki/Social_change" title="Social change">social change</a> was driven by conflict between opposing interests, by parties situated in the historical situation of their <a href="/wiki/Mode_of_production" title="Mode of production">mode of production</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-172">[172]</a></sup> This became the inspiration for the body of works known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conflict_theory" title="Conflict theory">conflict theory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner2005_244-1"><a href="#cite_note-Turner2005-244">[244]</a></sup> In his <a href="/wiki/Sociocultural_evolution" title="Sociocultural evolution">evolutionary</a> model of history, he argued that <a href="/wiki/Human_history" title="Human history">human history</a> began with free, productive and creative activities that was over time coerced and dehumanised, a trend most apparent under capitalism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-20-23_210-7"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-20-23-210">[210]</a></sup> Marx noted that this was not an intentional process, but rather due to the immanent logic of the current <a href="/wiki/Mode_of_production" title="Mode of production">mode of production</a> which demands more human labour (<a href="/wiki/Abstract_labour_and_concrete_labour" title="Abstract labour and concrete labour">abstract labour</a>) to reproduce the social relationships of capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>The organisation of society depends on <a href="/wiki/Means_of_production" title="Means of production">means of production</a>. The means of production are all things required to produce material goods, such as land, natural resources, and technology but not human labour. The <a href="/wiki/Relations_of_production" title="Relations of production">relations of production</a> are the social relationships people enter into as they acquire and use the means of production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner2005_244-2"><a href="#cite_note-Turner2005-244">[244]</a></sup> Together, these compose the <a href="/wiki/Mode_of_production" title="Mode of production">mode of production</a> and Marx distinguished historical eras in terms of modes of production. Marx differentiated between <a href="/wiki/Base_and_superstructure" title="Base and superstructure">base and superstructure</a>, where the base (or substructure) is the economic system and superstructure is the cultural and political system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner2005_244-3"><a href="#cite_note-Turner2005-244">[244]</a></sup> Marx regarded this mismatch between economic base and social <a href="/wiki/Base_and_superstructure" title="Base and superstructure">superstructure</a> as a major source of social disruption and conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner2005_244-4"><a href="#cite_note-Turner2005-244">[244]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite Marx's stress on the critique of capitalism and discussion of the new <a href="/wiki/Communist_society" title="Communist society">communist society</a> that should replace it, his explicit critique is guarded, as he saw it as an improved society compared to the past ones (<a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> and <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_78-8"><a href="#cite_note-sep-78">[78]</a></sup> Marx never clearly discusses issues of <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">justice</a>, but scholars agree that his work contained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logical_implication" title="Logical implication">implicit</a> discussion of those concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_78-9"><a href="#cite_note-sep-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner"
