Marx in Moscow, whose inscription reads: "<a href="/wiki/Workers_of_the_world,_unite!" title="Workers of the world, unite!">Proletarians of all countries, unite!</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:179px;max-width:179px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:132px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Murales_Rivera_-_Treppenhaus_7_Marx.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Murales_Rivera_-_Treppenhaus_7_Marx.jpg/177px-Murales_Rivera_-_Treppenhaus_7_Marx.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Murales_Rivera_-_Treppenhaus_7_Marx.jpg/266px-Murales_Rivera_-_Treppenhaus_7_Marx.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Murales_Rivera_-_Treppenhaus_7_Marx.jpg/354px-Murales_Rivera_-_Treppenhaus_7_Marx.jpg 2x" width="177"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Mural by <a href="/wiki/Diego_Rivera" title="Diego Rivera">Diego Rivera</a> showing Karl Marx, in the <a href="/wiki/National_Palace_(Mexico)" title="National Palace (Mexico)">National Palace</a> in Mexico City</div></div></div></div></div>
<p>Marx's view of capitalism was two-sided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_78-10"><a href="#cite_note-sep-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood1993_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wood1993-149">[149]</a></sup> On one hand, in the 19th century's deepest critique of the dehumanising aspects of this system he noted that defining features of capitalism include alienation, exploitation and recurring, cyclical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_depressions" title="Economic depressions">depressions</a> leading to mass unemployment. On the other hand, he characterised capitalism as "revolutionising, industrialising and universalising qualities of development, growth and progressivity" (by which Marx meant industrialisation, urbanisation, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Technological_progress" title="Technological progress">technological progress</a>, increased <a href="/wiki/Productivity" title="Productivity">productivity</a> and growth, <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_revolution" title="Scientific revolution">scientific revolution</a>) that are responsible for progress, at  in contrast to earlier forms of societies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_78-11"><a href="#cite_note-sep-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood1993_149-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wood1993-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-20-23_210-8"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-20-23-210">[210]</a></sup> Marx considered the capitalist class to be one of the most revolutionary in history because it constantly improved the means of production, more so than any other class in history and was responsible for the overthrow of <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark1998_214-2"><a href="#cite_note-Clark1998-214">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilbert2010_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gilbert2010-246">[246]</a></sup> Capitalism can stimulate considerable growth because the capitalist has an incentive to reinvest profits in new technologies and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capital_equipment" title="Capital equipment">capital equipment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-22_232-4"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-22-232">[232]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Marx, capitalists take advantage of the difference between the labour market and the market for whatever commodity the capitalist can produce. Marx observed that in practically every successful industry, input unit-costs are lower than output unit-prices. Marx called the difference "<a href="/wiki/Surplus_value" title="Surplus value">surplus value</a>" and argued that it was based on <a href="/wiki/Surplus_labour" title="Surplus labour">surplus labour</a>, the difference between what it costs to keep workers alive, and what they can produce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_78-12"><a href="#cite_note-sep-78">[78]</a></sup> Although Marx describes capitalists as <a href="/wiki/Vampire" title="Vampire">vampires</a> sucking worker's blood,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-20-23_210-9"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-20-23-210">[210]</a></sup> he notes that drawing profit is "by no means an injustice" since Marx, according to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Allen_Wood_(scholar)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Allen Wood (scholar) (page does not exist)">Allen Wood</a> "excludes any trans-epochal standpoint from which one can comment" on the morals of such particular arrangements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_78-13"><a href="#cite_note-sep-78">[78]</a></sup> Marx also noted that even the capitalists themselves cannot go against the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark1998_214-3"><a href="#cite_note-Clark1998-214">[214]</a></sup> The problem is the "cancerous cell" of <a href="/wiki/Capital_(economics)" title="Capital (economics)">capital</a>, understood not as property or equipment, but the social relations between workers and owners, (the selling and purchasing of labour power) – the societal system, or rather <a href="/wiki/Mode_of_production" title="Mode of production">mode of production</a>, in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark1998_214-4"><a href="#cite_note-Clark1998-214">[214]</a></sup>
</p><p>At the same time, Marx stressed that capitalism was unstable and prone to <a href="/wiki/Crisis_theory" title="Crisis theory">periodic crises</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wk_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-wk-92">[92]</a></sup> He suggested that over time capitalists would invest more and more in new technologies and less and less in labour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sep_78-14"><a href="#cite_note-sep-78">[78]</a></sup> Since Marx believed that profit derived from surplus value appropriated from labour, he concluded that the rate of profit would fall as the economy grows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-23_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-23-247">[247]</a></sup> Marx believed that increasingly severe crises would punctuate this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Business_cycles" title="Business cycles">cycle</a> of growth and collapse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-23_247-1"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-23-247">[247]</a></sup> Moreover, he believed that in the long-term, this process would enrich and <a href="/wiki/Empowerment" title="Empowerment">empower</a> the capitalist class and impoverish the proletariat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-23_247-2"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-23-247">[247]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark1998_214-5"><a href="#cite_note-Clark1998-214">[214]</a></sup> In section one of <i>The Communist Manifesto</i>, Marx describes <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a>, capitalism and the role internal social contradictions play in the historical process:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We see then: the means of production and of exchange, on whose foundation the bourgeoisie built itself up, were generated in feudal society. At a certain stage in the development of these means of production and of exchange, the conditions under which feudal society produced and exchanged ... the feudal relations of property became no longer compatible with the already developed productive forces; they became so many fetters. They had to be burst asunder; they were burst asunder. Into their place stepped free competition, accompanied by a social and political constitution adapted in it, and the economic and political sway of the bourgeois class. A similar movement is going on before our own eyes ... The productive forces at the disposal of society no longer tend to further the development of the conditions of bourgeois property; on the contrary, they have become too powerful for these conditions, by which they are fettered, and so soon as they overcome these fetters, they bring order into the whole of bourgeois society, endanger the existence of bourgeois property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manifesto_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-manifesto-3">[3]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="266" data-file-width="350" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg/220px-MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg/330px-MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MandK_Industrial_Revolution_1900.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Outside a factory in <a href="/wiki/Oldham" title="Oldham">Oldham</a>. Marx believed that industrial workers (the <a href="/wiki/Proletariat" title="Proletariat">proletariat</a>) would rise up around the world.</div></div></div>
<p>Marx believed that those structural contradictions within capitalism necessitate its end, giving way to socialism, or a post-capitalistic, communist society:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The development of Modern Industry, therefore, cuts from under its feet the very foundation on which the bourgeoisie produces and appropriates products. What the bourgeoisie, therefore, produces, above all, are its own grave-diggers. Its fall and the victory of the proletariat are equally inevitable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-manifesto_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-manifesto-3">[3]</a></sup></p></blockquote><p>
Thanks to various processes overseen by capitalism, such as urbanisation, the working class, the proletariat, should grow in numbers and develop <a href="/wiki/Class_consciousness" title="Class consciousness">class consciousness</a>, in time realising that they can and must change the system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-20-23_210-10"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-20-23-210">[210]</a></sup> Marx believed that if the proletariat were to seize the means of production, they would encourage social relations that would benefit everyone equally, abolishing exploiting class and introduce a system of production less vulnerable to cyclical crises.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-20-23_210-11"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-20-23-210">[210]</a></sup> Marx argued in <i><a href="/wiki/The_German_Ideology" title="The German Ideology">The German Ideology</a></i> that capitalism will end through the organised actions of an international working class: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Communism is for us not a state of affairs which is to be established, an ideal to which reality will have to adjust itself. We call communism the real movement which abolishes the present state of things. The conditions of this movement result from the premises now in existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elster1985_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-Elster1985-248">[248]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In this new society, the alienation would end and humans would be free to act without being bound by selling their labour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-23_247-3"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-23-247">[247]</a></sup> It would be a democratic society, enfranchising the entire population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark1998_214-6"><a href="#cite_note-Clark1998-214">[214]</a></sup> In such a <a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">utopian</a> world, there would also be little need for a state, whose goal was previously to enforce the alienation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-23_247-4"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-23-247">[247]</a></sup> Marx theorised that between capitalism and the establishment of a socialist/communist system, would exist a period of <a href="/wiki/Dictatorship_of_the_proletariat" title="Dictatorship of the proletariat">dictatorship of the proletariat</a> – where the working class holds political power and forcibly socialises the means of production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark1998_214-7"><a href="#cite_note-Clark1998-214">[214]</a></sup> As he wrote in his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critique_of_the_Gotha_Program" title="Critique of the Gotha Program">Critique of the Gotha Program</a></i>, "between capitalist and communist society there lies the period of the revolutionary transformation of the one into the other. Corresponding to this is also a political transition period in which the state can be nothing but the revolutionary dictatorship of the proletariat".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karl_Marx:Critique_of_the_Gotha_Programme_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karl_Marx:Critique_of_the_Gotha_Programme-249">[249]</a></sup> While he allowed for the possibility of peaceful transition in some countries with strong democratic institutional structures (such as Britain, the United States, and the Netherlands), he suggested that in other countries in which workers cannot "attain their goal by peaceful means" the "lever of our revolution must be force".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_relations">International relations</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karl_Marx_memorial.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="467" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Karl_Marx_memorial.jpg/200px-Karl_Marx_memorial.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Karl_Marx_memorial.jpg/300px-Karl_Marx_memorial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Karl_Marx_memorial.jpg/400px-Karl_Marx_memorial.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karl_Marx_memorial.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karl_Marx_Monument" title="Karl Marx Monument">Karl Marx Monument</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chemnitz" title="Chemnitz">Chemnitz</a> (known as <i>Karl-Marx-Stadt</i> from 1953 to 1990)</div></div></div>
<p>Marx viewed <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a> as the main counter-revolutionary threat to European revolutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anderson_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-anderson-251">[251]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_War" title="Crimean War">Crimean War</a>, Marx backed the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> and its allies Britain and France against Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anderson_251-1"><a href="#cite_note-anderson-251">[251]</a></sup> He was absolutely opposed to <a href="/wiki/Pan-Slavism" title="Pan-Slavism">Pan-Slavism</a>, viewing it as an instrument of Russian foreign policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anderson_251-2"><a href="#cite_note-anderson-251">[251]</a></sup> Marx had considered the <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavic</a> nations except <a href="/wiki/Poles" title="Poles">Poles</a> as 'counter-revolutionary'. Marx and Engels published in the <i>Neue Rheinische Zeitung</i> in February 1849:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>To the sentimental phrases about brotherhood which we are being offered here on behalf of the most counter-revolutionary nations of Europe, we reply that hatred of Russians was and still is the primary revolutionary passion among Germans; that since the <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848" title="Revolutions of 1848">revolution</a> [of 1848] hatred of Czechs and Croats has been added, and that only by the most determined use of terror against these Slav peoples can we, jointly with the Poles and Magyars, safeguard the revolution. We know where the enemies of the revolution are concentrated, <i>viz</i>. in Russia and the Slav regions of Austria, and no fine phrases, no allusions to an undefined democratic future for these countries can deter us from treating our enemies as enemies. Then there will be a struggle, an "inexorable life-and-death struggle", against those Slavs who betray the revolution; an annihilating fight and ruthless terror – not in the interests of Germany, but in the interests of the revolution!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Marx and Engels sympathised with the <a href="/wiki/Narodniks" title="Narodniks">Narodnik</a> revolutionaries of the 1860s and 1870s. When the <a href="/wiki/Narodnaya_Volya" title="Narodnaya Volya">Russian revolutionaries</a> assassinated Tsar <a href="/wiki/Alexander_II_of_Russia" title="Alexander II of Russia">Alexander II of Russia</a>, Marx expressed the hope that the assassination foreshadowed 'the formation of a Russian commune'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> Marx supported the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Russian_Wars" title="Polish–Russian Wars">Polish uprisings</a> against tsarist Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-anderson_251-3"><a href="#cite_note-anderson-251">[251]</a></sup> He said in a speech in London in 1867:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In the first place the policy of Russia is changeless... Its methods, its tactics, its manoeuvres may change, but the polar star of its policy – world domination – is a fixed star. In our times only a civilised government ruling over barbarian masses can hatch out such a plan and execute it. ... There is but one alternative for Europe. Either Asiatic barbarism, under Muscovite direction, will burst around its head like an avalanche, or else it must re-establish Poland, thus putting twenty million heroes between itself and Asia and gaining a breathing spell for the accomplishment of its social regeneration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:India-7669_-_Flickr_-_archer10_(Dennis).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2448" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/India-7669_-_Flickr_-_archer10_%28Dennis%29.jpg/170px-India-7669_-_Flickr_-_archer10_%28Dennis%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/India-7669_-_Flickr_-_archer10_%28Dennis%29.jpg/255px-India-7669_-_Flickr_-_archer10_%28Dennis%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/India-7669_-_Flickr_-_archer10_%28Dennis%29.jpg/340px-India-7669_-_Flickr_-_archer10_%28Dennis%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:India-7669_-_Flickr_-_archer10_(Dennis).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Communist_Party_of_India_(Marxist)" title="Communist Party of India (Marxist)">CPI(M)</a> mural in <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a></div></div></div>
<p>Marx supported the cause of <a href="/wiki/Irish_Home_Rule_movement" title="Irish Home Rule movement">Irish independence</a>. In 1867, he wrote Engels: "I used to think the separation of Ireland from England impossible. I now think it inevitable. The English working class will never accomplish anything until it has got rid of Ireland. ... English reaction in England had its roots ... in the subjugation of Ireland."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marx spent some time in <a href="/wiki/French_Algeria" title="French Algeria">French Algeria</a>, which had been <a href="/wiki/French_conquest_of_Algeria" title="French conquest of Algeria">invaded</a> and made a <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">French colony</a> in 1830, and had the opportunity to observe life in colonial North Africa. He wrote about the colonial justice system, in which "a form of torture has been used (and this happens 'regularly') to extract confessions from the Arabs; naturally it is done (like the English in India) by the 'police'; the judge is supposed to know nothing at all about it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alahram_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-alahram-256">[256]</a></sup> Marx was surprised by the arrogance of many <a href="/wiki/Pied-Noir" title="Pied-Noir">European settlers</a> in Algiers and wrote in a letter: "when a European colonist dwells among the 'lesser breeds,' either as a settler or even on business, he generally regards himself as even more inviolable than handsome William I [a Prussian king]. Still, when it comes to bare-faced arrogance and presumptuousness vis-à-vis the 'lesser breeds,' the British and Dutch outdo the French."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alahram_256-1"><a href="#cite_note-alahram-256">[256]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Stanford_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy" title="Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy">Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</a></i>: "Marx's analysis of colonialism as a progressive force bringing modernization to a backward feudal society sounds like a transparent rationalization for foreign domination. His account of British domination, however, reflects the same ambivalence that he shows towards capitalism in Europe. In both cases, Marx recognizes the immense suffering brought about during the transition from feudal to bourgeois society while insisting that the transition is both necessary and ultimately progressive. He argues that the penetration of foreign commerce will cause a social revolution in India."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marx discussed <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British</a> <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">colonial rule</a> in <a href="/wiki/Colonial_India" title="Colonial India">India</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Herald_Tribune" title="New York Herald Tribune">New York Herald Tribune</a></i> in June 1853:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There cannot remain any doubt but that the misery inflicted by the British on Hindostan [India] is of an essentially different and infinitely more intensive kind than all Hindostan had to suffer before. England has broken down the entire framework of Indian society, without any symptoms of reconstitution yet appearing... [however], we must not forget that these idyllic village communities, inoffensive though they may appear, had always been the solid foundation of Oriental despotism, that they restrained the human mind within the smallest possible compass, making it the unresisting tool of superstition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alahram_256-2"><a href="#cite_note-alahram-256">[256]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marx_und_Engels_Denkmal,_alter_Platz_-_panoramio.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Marx_und_Engels_Denkmal%2C_alter_Platz_-_panoramio.jpg/220px-Marx_und_Engels_Denkmal%2C_alter_Platz_-_panoramio.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Marx_und_Engels_Denkmal%2C_alter_Platz_-_panoramio.jpg/330px-Marx_und_Engels_Denkmal%2C_alter_Platz_-_panoramio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Marx_und_Engels_Denkmal%2C_alter_Platz_-_panoramio.jpg/440px-Marx_und_Engels_Denkmal%2C_alter_Platz_-_panoramio.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marx_und_Engels_Denkmal,_alter_Platz_-_panoramio.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Karl Marx and Friedrich Engels monument in <a href="/wiki/Marx-Engels_Forum" title="Marx-Engels Forum">Marx-Engels Forum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berlin-Mitte" title="Berlin-Mitte">Berlin-Mitte</a>, Germany</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karl-Marx-Statue_in_Trier.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="902" decoding="async" height="386" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Karl-Marx-Statue_in_Trier.jpg/170px-Karl-Marx-Statue_in_Trier.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Karl-Marx-Statue_in_Trier.jpg/255px-Karl-Marx-Statue_in_Trier.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Karl-Marx-Statue_in_Trier.jpg/340px-Karl-Marx-Statue_in_Trier.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karl-Marx-Statue_in_Trier.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Karl Marx statue in Trier, Germany</div></div></div>
<p>Marx's ideas have had a profound impact on world politics and intellectual thought.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-23-24_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-23-24-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manchester_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Manchester-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allan2010_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allan2010-260">[260]</a></sup> Followers of Marx have often debated among themselves over how to interpret Marx's writings and apply his concepts to the modern world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndersenKaspersen2000_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-AndersenKaspersen2000-261">[261]</a></sup> The legacy of Marx's thought has become contested between numerous tendencies, each of which sees itself as Marx's most accurate interpreter. In the political realm, these tendencies include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_theories" title="Political theories">political theories</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Leninism" title="Leninism">Leninism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marxism%E2%80%93Leninism" title="Marxism–Leninism">Marxism–Leninism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trotskyism" title="Trotskyism">Trotskyism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maoism" title="Maoism">Maoism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luxemburgism" title="Luxemburgism">Luxemburgism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_Marxism" title="Libertarian Marxism">libertarian Marxism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndersenKaspersen2000_261-1"><a href="#cite_note-AndersenKaspersen2000-261">[261]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Open_Marxism" title="Open Marxism">Open Marxism</a>, Various currents have also developed in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academic_Marxism" title="Academic Marxism">academic Marxism</a>, often under influence of other views, resulting in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Structuralist_Marxism" title="Structuralist Marxism">structuralist Marxism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Historical_materialism" title="Historical materialism">historical materialism</a>, phenomenological Marxism, <a href="/wiki/Analytical_Marxism" title="Analytical Marxism">analytical Marxism</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegelian_Marxism" title="Hegelian Marxism">Hegelian Marxism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AndersenKaspersen2000_261-2"><a href="#cite_note-AndersenKaspersen2000-261">[261]</a></sup>
</p><p>From an academic perspective, Marx's work contributed to the birth of modern sociology. He has been cited as one of the 19th century's three masters of the "<a href="/wiki/Hermeneutics_of_suspicion" title="Hermeneutics of suspicion">school of suspicion</a>", alongside <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">Sigmund Freud</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[262]</a></sup> and as one of the three principal architects of modern <a href="/wiki/Social_science" title="Social science">social science</a> along with <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Durkheim" title="Émile Durkheim">Émile Durkheim</a> and <a href="/wiki/Max_Weber" title="Max Weber">Max Weber</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plato.stanford.edu_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-plato.stanford.edu-263">[263]</a></sup> In contrast to other philosophers, Marx offered theories that could often be tested with the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-23-24_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-23-24-5">[5]</a></sup> Both Marx and <a href="/wiki/Auguste_Comte" title="Auguste Comte">Auguste Comte</a> set out to develop scientifically justified ideologies in the wake of European <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secularisation" title="Secularisation">secularisation</a> and new developments in the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_history" title="Philosophy of history">philosophies of history</a> and science. Working in the Hegelian tradition, Marx rejected Comtean <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sociological_positivism" title="Sociological positivism">sociological positivism</a> in an attempt to develop a <i>science of society</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calhoun2002-19_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calhoun2002-19-264">[264]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karl_L%C3%B6with" title="Karl Löwith">Karl Löwith</a> considered Marx and <a href="/wiki/S%C3%B8ren_Kierkegaard" title="Søren Kierkegaard">Søren Kierkegaard</a> to be the two greatest Hegelian philosophical successors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup> In modern <a href="/wiki/Sociological_theory" title="Sociological theory">sociological theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marxist_sociology" title="Marxist sociology">Marxist sociology</a> is recognised as one of the main classical perspectives. <a href="/wiki/Isaiah_Berlin" title="Isaiah Berlin">Isaiah Berlin</a> considers Marx the true founder of modern sociology "in so far as anyone can claim the title".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[266]</a></sup> Beyond social science, he has also had a lasting legacy in philosophy, literature, the arts and the humanities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singer_1980._p._1_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-Singer_1980._p._1-267">[267]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[268]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[269]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Communist_countries.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="440" data-file-width="860" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Communist_countries.svg/300px-Communist_countries.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Communist_countries.svg/450px-Communist_countries.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Communist_countries.svg/600px-Communist_countries.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Communist_countries.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of countries that declared themselves to be socialist states under the <a href="/wiki/Marxism%E2%80%93Leninism" title="Marxism–Leninism">Marxist–Leninist</a> or <a href="/wiki/Maoism" title="Maoism">Maoist</a> definition between 1979 and 1983, which marked the greatest territorial extent of <a href="/wiki/List_of_socialist_states" title="List of socialist states">socialist states</a></div></div></div>
<p>Social theorists of the 20th and 21st centuries have pursued two main strategies in response to Marx. One move has been to reduce it to its analytical core, known as analytical Marxism. Another, more common move has been to dilute the explanatory claims of Marx's social theory and emphasise the "relative autonomy" of aspects of social and economic life not directly related to Marx's central narrative of interaction between the development of the "forces of production" and the succession of "modes of production". This has been the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-Marxist" title="Neo-Marxist">neo-Marxist</a> theorising adopted by historians inspired by Marx's social theory such as <a href="/wiki/E._P._Thompson" title="E. P. Thompson">E. P. Thompson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eric_Hobsbawm" title="Eric Hobsbawm">Eric Hobsbawm</a>. It has also been a line of thinking pursued by thinkers and activists such as <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Gramsci" title="Antonio Gramsci">Antonio Gramsci</a> who have sought to understand the opportunities and the difficulties of transformative political practice, seen in the light of Marxist social theory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[271]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[274]</a></sup> Marx's ideas would also have a profound influence on subsequent artists and art history, with avant-garde movements across literature, visual art, music, film, and theatre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[275]</a></sup>
</p><p>Politically, Marx's legacy is more complex. Throughout the 20th century, revolutions in dozens of countries labelled themselves "Marxist"—most notably the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Russian Revolution</a>, which led to the founding of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-April_Thesis_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-April_Thesis-276">[276]</a></sup> Major world leaders including <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-April_Thesis_276-1"><a href="#cite_note-April_Thesis-276">[276]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mao_Zedong" title="Mao Zedong">Mao Zedong</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-277"><a href="#cite_note-277">[277]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Salvador_Allende" title="Salvador Allende">Salvador Allende</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[279]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Josip_Broz_Tito" title="Josip Broz Tito">Josip Broz Tito</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[280]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah" title="Kwame Nkrumah">Kwame Nkrumah</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[281]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru" title="Jawaharlal Nehru">Jawaharlal Nehru</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[282]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[283]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Xi_Jinping" title="Xi Jinping">Xi Jinping</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euronews_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-euronews-284">[284]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jean-Claude_Juncker" title="Jean-Claude Juncker">Jean-Claude Juncker</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-euronews_284-1"><a href="#cite_note-euronews-284">[284]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-independent_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-independent-285">[285]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Sankara" title="Thomas Sankara">Thomas Sankara</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[286]</a></sup> have all cited Marx as an influence. Beyond where Marxist revolutions took place, Marx's ideas have informed political parties worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup> In countries associated with Marxism, some events have led political opponents to blame Marx for millions of deaths,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[288]</a></sup> while others argue for a distinction between the legacy and influence of Marx specifically, and the legacy and influence of those who have shaped his ideas for political purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[289]</a></sup> Arthur Lipow describes Marx and his collaborator <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a> as "the founders of modern revolutionary democratic socialism."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup>
</p><p>Marx remains both relevant and controversial. In May 2018, to mark the bicentenary of his birth, a 4.5m statue of him by leading Chinese sculptor <a href="/wiki/Wu_Weishan" title="Wu Weishan">Wu Weishan</a> and donated by the Chinese government was unveiled in his birthplace of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trier,_Germany" title="Trier, Germany">Trier</a>. The then-<a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> president Jean-Claude Juncker defended Marx's memory, saying that today Marx "stands for things which he is not responsible for and which he didn't cause because many of the things he wrote down were redrafted into the opposite".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-independent_285-1"><a href="#cite_note-independent-285">[285]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[291]</a></sup>
In 2017, a feature film, titled <i><a href="/wiki/The_Young_Karl_Marx" title="The Young Karl Marx">The Young Karl Marx</a></i>, featuring Marx, his wife <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jenny_Marx" title="Jenny Marx">Jenny Marx</a>, and Engels, among other revolutionaries and intellectuals prior to the <a href="/wiki/Revolutions_of_1848" title="Revolutions of 1848">Revolutions of 1848</a>, received good reviews for both its historical accuracy and its brio in dealing with intellectual life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[292]</a></sup> Another fictional representation to coincide with the bicentenary was <a href="/wiki/Jason_Barker" title="Jason Barker">Jason Barker</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Marx_Returns" title="Marx Returns">Marx Returns</a></i> which, despite being "[c]urious, funny, perplexing, and irreverent", according to philosopher <a href="/wiki/Ray_Brassier" title="Ray Brassier">Ray Brassier</a> "casts unexpected light on Marx's thought."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Book_Depository_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-Book_Depository-293">[293]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-166"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-166">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFMarx">Marx. <a class="external text" href="https://www.marxists.org/archive/marx/works/1859/critique-pol-economy/appx1.htm#205" rel="nofollow">"Economic Manuscripts: Appendix I: Production, Consumption, Distribution, Exchange"</a>. <i>www.marxists.org</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">28 March</span> 2022</span>. <q>[...] The solitary and isolated hunter or fisherman, who serves Adam Smith and Ricardo as a starting point, is one of the unimaginative fantasies of eighteenth-century romances a la Robinson Crusoe; and despite the assertions of social historians, these by no means signify simply a reaction against over-refinement and reversion to a misconceived natural life. [...] This is an illusion and nothing but the aesthetic illusion of the small and big Robinsonades. It is, on the contrary, the anticipation of “bourgeois society,” which began to evolve in the sixteenth century and in the eighteenth century made giant strides towards maturity. The individual in this society of free competition seems to be rid of natural ties, etc., which made him an appurtenance of a particular, limited aggregation of human beings in previous historical epochs. The prophets of the eighteenth century, on whose shoulders Adam Smith and Ricardo were still wholly standing, envisaged this 18th-century individual – a product of the dissolution of feudal society on the one hand and of the new productive forces evolved since the sixteenth century on the other – as an ideal whose existence belonged to the past. They saw this individual not as an historical result, but as the starting point of history<br/>[...]<br/>Labour seems to be a very simple category. The notion of labour in this universal form, as labour in general, is also extremely old. Nevertheless “labour” in this simplicity is economically considered just as modern a category as the relations which give rise to this simple abstraction.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=www.marxists.org&amp;rft.atitle=Economic+Manuscripts%3A+Appendix+I%3A+Production%2C+Consumption%2C+Distribution%2C+Exchange&amp;rft.au=Marx&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.marxists.org%2Farchive%2Fmarx%2Fworks%2F1859%2Fcritique-pol-economy%2Fappx1.htm%23205&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKarl+Marx"></span></span>
</li>

Marx defined criticism as the "radical negation of social reality."</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Classical+sociological+theory&amp;rft.place=Chichester%2C+West+Sussex&amp;rft.pages=138&amp;rft.edition=3rd&amp;rft.pub=John+Wiley+%26+Sons&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F794037359&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-470-65567-2&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.worldcat.org%2Foclc%2F794037359&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKarl+Marx"></span><span class="cs1-maint citation-comment"><code class="cs1-code">{{<a href="/wiki/Template:Cite_book" title="Template:Cite book">cite book</a>}}</code>:  CS1 maint: others (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_others" title="Category:CS1 maint: others">link</a>)</span></span>

<li id="cite_note-186"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-186">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFLafargue1972"><a href="/wiki/Paul_Lafargue" title="Paul Lafargue">Lafargue, Paul</a> (1972).  <a href="/wiki/Marx%E2%80%93Engels%E2%80%93Lenin_Institute" title="Marx–Engels–Lenin Institute">Marx–Engels–Lenin Institute</a> (ed.). <a class="external text" href="https://www.marxists.org/archive/lafargue/1890/xx/marx.htm" rel="nofollow"><i><span></span></i>Reminiscences of Marx<i> (September 1890)</i></a>. Progress Publishers. <q>He was a loving, gentle and indulgent father. [...] There was never even a trace of the bossy parent in his relations with his daughters, whose love for him was extraordinary. He never gave them an order, but asked them to do what he wished as a favour or made them feel that they should not do what he wanted to forbid them. And yet a father could seldom have had more docile children than he.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Reminiscences+of+Marx+%28September+1890%29&amp;rft.pub=Progress+Publishers&amp;rft.date=1972&amp;rft.aulast=Lafargue&amp;rft.aufirst=Paul&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.marxists.org%2Farchive%2Flafargue%2F1890%2Fxx%2Fmarx.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKarl+Marx"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Karl_Marx:Critique_of_the_Gotha_Programme-249"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Karl_Marx:Critique_of_the_Gotha_Programme_249-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.marxists.org/archive/marx/works/1875/gotha/ch04.htm" rel="nofollow">"Critique of the Gotha Programme-- IV"</a>. <i>marxists.org</i>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20190702071606/https://www.marxists.org/archive/marx/works/1875/gotha/ch04.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 2 July 2019<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">16 May</span> 2019</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=marxists.org&amp;rft.atitle=Critique+of+the+Gotha+Programme--+IV&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.marxists.org%2Farchive%2Fmarx%2Fworks%2F1875%2Fgotha%2Fch04.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKarl+Marx"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-250"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-250">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="https://www.marxists.org/archive/marx/works/1872/09/08.htm" rel="nofollow">La Liberté Speech</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20180716015355/https://www.marxists.org/archive/marx/works/1872/09/08.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 16 July 2018 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> delivered by Karl Marx on 8 September 1872, in Amsterdam. "You know that the institutions, mores, and traditions of various countries must be taken into consideration, and we do not deny that there are countries – such as America, England, and if I were more familiar with your institutions, I would perhaps also add Holland – where the workers can attain their goal by peaceful means. This being the case, we must also recognise the fact that in most countries on the Continent the lever of our revolution must be force; it is force to which we must some day appeal to erect the rule of labour."</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-290"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-290">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Lipow, Arthur (1991). <i>Authoritarian Socialism in America: Edward Bellamy and the Nationalist Movement</i>. University of California Press. p. 1. <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-520-07543-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-520-07543-6">978-0-520-07543-6</a>. "<span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>We are not among those communists who are out to destroy personal liberty, who wish to turn the world into one huge barrack or into a gigantic workhouse. There certainly are some communists who, with an easy conscience, refuse to countenance personal liberty and would like to shuffle it out of the world because they consider that it is a hindrance to complete harmony. But we have no desire to exchange freedom for equality. We are convinced ... that in no social order will freedom be assured as in a society based upon communal ownership.' Thus wrote the editors of the <i>Journal</i> of the Communist League in 1847, under the direct influence of the founders of modern revolutionary democratic socialism, Karl Marx and Frederick Engels."</span>
</li>

<li>The <i>Collected Works</i> of Marx and Engels, in English translation and in 50 volumes, are published in London by Lawrence &amp; Wishart and in New York by International Publishers. (These volumes were at one time put online by the <a href="/wiki/Marxists_Internet_Archive" title="Marxists Internet Archive">Marxists Internet Archive</a>, until the original publishers objected on copyright grounds: <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.marxists.org/archive/marx/works/cw/index.htm" rel="nofollow">"Marx/Engels Collected Works"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Marxists_Internet_Archive" title="Marxists Internet Archive">Marxists Internet Archive</a></i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">3 March</span> 2018</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Marxists+Internet+Archive&amp;rft.atitle=Marx%2FEngels+Collected+Works&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.marxists.org%2Farchive%2Fmarx%2Fworks%2Fcw%2Findex.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AKarl+Marx"></span>) They are available online and searchable, for purchase or through subscribing libraries, in the "<a class="external text" href="https://alexanderstreet.com/products/social-theory" rel="nofollow">Social Theory</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20180303164706/https://alexanderstreet.com/products/social-theory" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 3 March 2018 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>" collection published by <a class="external text" href="https://alexanderstreet-com.simsrad.net.ocs.mq.edu.au/page/history-imprints" rel="nofollow">Alexander Street Press</a> in collaboration with the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a>.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/p003k9jg" rel="nofollow">"Marx"</a>, <a href="/wiki/BBC_Radio_4" title="BBC Radio 4">BBC Radio 4</a> discussion with Anthony Grayling, Francis Wheen &amp; Gareth Stedman Jones (<i><a href="/wiki/In_Our_Time_(radio_series)" title="In Our Time (radio series)">In Our Time</a></i>, 14 July 2005)</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.oldmagazinearticles.com/article-summary/das-kapital-book-review#.XtAB0mhKizk" rel="nofollow">The 1887 <i>NY Times</i> review of <i>Das Kapital</i></a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://purl.org/pressemappe20/folder/pe/011971" rel="nofollow">Newspaper clippings about Karl Marx</a> in the <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Press_Archives" title="20th Century Press Archives">20th Century Press Archives</a> of the <a href="/wiki/German_National_Library_of_Economics" title="German National Library of Economics">ZBW</a></li></ul>
